    85	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 1 PETER BREAKS THROUGH
    86	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
    87	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAll children, except one, grow up. They soon know that they will grow
    88	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tup, and the way Wendy knew was this. One day when she was two years old
    89	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe was playing in a garden, and she plucked another flower and ran with
    90	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit to her mother. I suppose she must have looked rather delightful, for
    91	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMrs. Darling put her hand to her heart and cried, “Oh, why can’t you
    92	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tremain like this for ever!” This was all that passed between them on
    93	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe subject, but henceforth Wendy knew that she must grow up. You always
    94	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tknow after you are two. Two is the beginning of the end.
    95	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
    96	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOf course they lived at 14 [their house number on their street], and
    97	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tuntil Wendy came her mother was the chief one. She was a lovely lady,
    98	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith a romantic mind and such a sweet mocking mouth. Her romantic
    99	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmind was like the tiny boxes, one within the other, that come from the
   100	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpuzzling East, however many you discover there is always one more; and
   101	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther sweet mocking mouth had one kiss on it that Wendy could never get,
   102	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthough there it was, perfectly conspicuous in the right-hand corner.
   103	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   104	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe way Mr. Darling won her was this: the many gentlemen who had been
   105	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tboys when she was a girl discovered simultaneously that they loved her,
   106	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand they all ran to her house to propose to her except Mr. Darling, who
   107	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttook a cab and nipped in first, and so he got her. He got all of her,
   108	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\texcept the innermost box and the kiss. He never knew about the box, and
   109	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin time he gave up trying for the kiss. Wendy thought Napoleon could
   110	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thave got it, but I can picture him trying, and then going off in a
   111	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpassion, slamming the door.
   112	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   113	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMr. Darling used to boast to Wendy that her mother not only loved him
   114	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbut respected him. He was one of those deep ones who know about stocks
   115	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand shares. Of course no one really knows, but he quite seemed to know,
   116	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand he often said stocks were up and shares were down in a way that
   117	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould have made any woman respect him.
   118	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   119	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMrs. Darling was married in white, and at first she kept the books
   120	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tperfectly, almost gleefully, as if it were a game, not so much as a
   121	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBrussels sprout was missing; but by and by whole cauliflowers dropped
   122	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tout, and instead of them there were pictures of babies without faces.
   123	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe drew them when she should have been totting up. They were Mrs.
   124	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDarling’s guesses.
   125	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   126	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy came first, then John, then Michael.
   127	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   128	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFor a week or two after Wendy came it was doubtful whether they would
   129	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbe able to keep her, as she was another mouth to feed. Mr. Darling was
   130	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfrightfully proud of her, but he was very honourable, and he sat on the
   131	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tedge of Mrs. Darling’s bed, holding her hand and calculating expenses,
   132	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhile she looked at him imploringly. She wanted to risk it, come what
   133	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmight, but that was not his way; his way was with a pencil and a piece
   134	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof paper, and if she confused him with suggestions he had to begin at
   135	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe beginning again.
   136	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   137	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Now don’t interrupt,” he would beg of her.
   138	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   139	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I have one pound seventeen here, and two and six at the office; I can
   140	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcut off my coffee at the office, say ten shillings, making two nine
   141	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand six, with your eighteen and three makes three nine seven, with five
   142	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnaught naught in my cheque-book makes eight nine seven--who is that
   143	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmoving?--eight nine seven, dot and carry seven--don’t speak, my own--and
   144	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe pound you lent to that man who came to the door--quiet, child--dot
   145	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand carry child--there, you’ve done it!--did I say nine nine seven? yes,
   146	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tI said nine nine seven; the question is, can we try it for a year on
   147	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnine nine seven?”
   148	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   149	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Of course we can, George,” she cried. But she was prejudiced in Wendy’s
   150	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfavour, and he was really the grander character of the two.
   151	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   152	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Remember mumps,” he warned her almost threateningly, and off he went
   153	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tagain. “Mumps one pound, that is what I have put down, but I daresay
   154	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit will be more like thirty shillings--don’t speak--measles one five,
   155	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tGerman measles half a guinea, makes two fifteen six--don’t waggle your
   156	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfinger--whooping-cough, say fifteen shillings”--and so on it went, and
   157	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit added up differently each time; but at last Wendy just got through,
   158	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith mumps reduced to twelve six, and the two kinds of measles treated
   159	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tas one.
   160	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   161	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThere was the same excitement over John, and Michael had even a narrower
   162	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsqueak; but both were kept, and soon, you might have seen the three of
   163	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem going in a row to Miss Fulsom’s Kindergarten school, accompanied by
   164	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir nurse.
   165	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   166	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMrs. Darling loved to have everything just so, and Mr. Darling had a
   167	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpassion for being exactly like his neighbours; so, of course, they had
   168	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta nurse. As they were poor, owing to the amount of milk the children
   169	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdrank, this nurse was a prim Newfoundland dog, called Nana, who had
   170	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbelonged to no one in particular until the Darlings engaged her. She had
   171	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\talways thought children important, however, and the Darlings had become
   172	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tacquainted with her in Kensington Gardens, where she spent most of her
   173	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tspare time peeping into perambulators, and was much hated by careless
   174	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnursemaids, whom she followed to their homes and complained of to their
   175	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmistresses. She proved to be quite a treasure of a nurse. How thorough
   176	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe was at bath-time, and up at any moment of the night if one of her
   177	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcharges made the slightest cry. Of course her kennel was in the nursery.
   178	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe had a genius for knowing when a cough is a thing to have no patience
   179	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith and when it needs stocking around your throat. She believed to her
   180	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlast day in old-fashioned remedies like rhubarb leaf, and made sounds of
   181	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcontempt over all this new-fangled talk about germs, and so on. It was a
   182	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlesson in propriety to see her escorting the children to school, walking
   183	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsedately by their side when they were well behaved, and butting them
   184	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tback into line if they strayed. On John’s footer [in England soccer
   185	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas called football, “footer” for short] days she never once forgot his
   186	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsweater, and she usually carried an umbrella in her mouth in case of
   187	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\train. There is a room in the basement of Miss Fulsom’s school where the
   188	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnurses wait. They sat on forms, while Nana lay on the floor, but that
   189	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas the only difference. They affected to ignore her as of an inferior
   190	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsocial status to themselves, and she despised their light talk. She
   191	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tresented visits to the nursery from Mrs. Darling’s friends, but if they
   192	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdid come she first whipped off Michael’s pinafore and put him into the
   193	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tone with blue braiding, and smoothed out Wendy and made a dash at John’s
   194	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thair.
   195	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   196	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNo nursery could possibly have been conducted more correctly, and
   197	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMr. Darling knew it, yet he sometimes wondered uneasily whether the
   198	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tneighbours talked.
   199	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   200	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe had his position in the city to consider.
   201	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   202	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNana also troubled him in another way. He had sometimes a feeling that
   203	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe did not admire him. “I know she admires you tremendously, George,”
   204	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t Mrs. Darling would assure him, and then she would sign to the children
   205	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto be specially nice to father. Lovely dances followed, in which the
   206	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tonly other servant, Liza, was sometimes allowed to join. Such a midget
   207	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe looked in her long skirt and maid’s cap, though she had sworn, when
   208	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tengaged, that she would never see ten again. The gaiety of those romps!
   209	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAnd gayest of all was Mrs. Darling, who would pirouette so wildly that
   210	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tall you could see of her was the kiss, and then if you had dashed at her
   211	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyou might have got it. There never was a simpler happier family until
   212	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe coming of Peter Pan.
   213	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   214	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMrs. Darling first heard of Peter when she was tidying up her children’s
   215	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tminds. It is the nightly custom of every good mother after her children
   216	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tare asleep to rummage in their minds and put things straight for next
   217	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmorning, repacking into their proper places the many articles that have
   218	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twandered during the day. If you could keep awake (but of course you
   219	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcan’t) you would see your own mother doing this, and you would find it
   220	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tvery interesting to watch her. It is quite like tidying up drawers. You
   221	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould see her on her knees, I expect, lingering humorously over some of
   222	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyour contents, wondering where on earth you had picked this thing up,
   223	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmaking discoveries sweet and not so sweet, pressing this to her cheek as
   224	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tif it were as nice as a kitten, and hurriedly stowing that out of sight.
   225	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhen you wake in the morning, the naughtiness and evil passions with
   226	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhich you went to bed have been folded up small and placed at the bottom
   227	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof your mind and on the top, beautifully aired, are spread out your
   228	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tprettier thoughts, ready for you to put on.
   229	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   230	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tI don’t know whether you have ever seen a map of a person’s mind.
   231	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDoctors sometimes draw maps of other parts of you, and your own map can
   232	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbecome intensely interesting, but catch them trying to draw a map of a
   233	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tchild’s mind, which is not only confused, but keeps going round all
   234	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe time. There are zigzag lines on it, just like your temperature on a
   235	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcard, and these are probably roads in the island, for the Neverland is
   236	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\talways more or less an island, with astonishing splashes of colour here
   237	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand there, and coral reefs and rakish-looking craft in the offing, and
   238	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsavages and lonely lairs, and gnomes who are mostly tailors, and caves
   239	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthrough which a river runs, and princes with six elder brothers, and a
   240	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thut fast going to decay, and one very small old lady with a hooked nose.
   241	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt would be an easy map if that were all, but there is also first day
   242	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat school, religion, fathers, the round pond, needle-work, murders,
   243	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thangings, verbs that take the dative, chocolate pudding day, getting
   244	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tinto braces, say ninety-nine, three-pence for pulling out your tooth
   245	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyourself, and so on, and either these are part of the island or they are
   246	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tanother map showing through, and it is all rather confusing, especially
   247	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tas nothing will stand still.
   248	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   249	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOf course the Neverlands vary a good deal. John’s, for instance, had a
   250	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlagoon with flamingoes flying over it at which John was shooting, while
   251	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMichael, who was very small, had a flamingo with lagoons flying over
   252	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit. John lived in a boat turned upside down on the sands, Michael in
   253	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta wigwam, Wendy in a house of leaves deftly sewn together. John had no
   254	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfriends, Michael had friends at night, Wendy had a pet wolf forsaken by
   255	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tits parents, but on the whole the Neverlands have a family resemblance,
   256	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand if they stood still in a row you could say of them that they have
   257	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\teach other’s nose, and so forth. On these magic shores children at play
   258	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tare for ever beaching their coracles [simple boat]. We too have been
   259	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthere; we can still hear the sound of the surf, though we shall land no
   260	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmore.
   261	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   262	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOf all delectable islands the Neverland is the snuggest and most
   263	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcompact, not large and sprawly, you know, with tedious distances between
   264	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tone adventure and another, but nicely crammed. When you play at it by
   265	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tday with the chairs and table-cloth, it is not in the least alarming,
   266	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbut in the two minutes before you go to sleep it becomes very real. That
   267	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tis why there are night-lights.
   268	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   269	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOccasionally in her travels through her children’s minds Mrs. Darling
   270	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfound things she could not understand, and of these quite the most
   271	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tperplexing was the word Peter. She knew of no Peter, and yet he was
   272	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\there and there in John and Michael’s minds, while Wendy’s began to be
   273	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tscrawled all over with him. The name stood out in bolder letters than
   274	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tany of the other words, and as Mrs. Darling gazed she felt that it had
   275	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tan oddly cocky appearance.
   276	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   277	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes, he is rather cocky,” Wendy admitted with regret. Her mother had
   278	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbeen questioning her.
   279	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   280	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“But who is he, my pet?”
   281	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   282	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“He is Peter Pan, you know, mother.”
   283	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   284	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAt first Mrs. Darling did not know, but after thinking back into her
   285	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tchildhood she just remembered a Peter Pan who was said to live with the
   286	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfairies. There were odd stories about him, as that when children died he
   287	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twent part of the way with them, so that they should not be frightened.
   288	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe had believed in him at the time, but now that she was married and
   289	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfull of sense she quite doubted whether there was any such person.
   290	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   291	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Besides,” she said to Wendy, “he would be grown up by this time.”
   292	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   293	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Oh no, he isn’t grown up,” Wendy assured her confidently, “and he is
   294	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tjust my size.” She meant that he was her size in both mind and body; she
   295	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdidn’t know how she knew, she just knew it.
   296	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   297	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMrs. Darling consulted Mr. Darling, but he smiled pooh-pooh. “Mark my
   298	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twords,” he said, “it is some nonsense Nana has been putting into their
   299	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\theads; just the sort of idea a dog would have. Leave it alone, and it
   300	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twill blow over.”
   301	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   302	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBut it would not blow over and soon the troublesome boy gave Mrs.
   303	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDarling quite a shock.
   304	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   305	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChildren have the strangest adventures without being troubled by them.
   306	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFor instance, they may remember to mention, a week after the event
   307	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thappened, that when they were in the wood they had met their dead
   308	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfather and had a game with him. It was in this casual way that Wendy one
   309	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmorning made a disquieting revelation. Some leaves of a tree had been
   310	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfound on the nursery floor, which certainly were not there when the
   311	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tchildren went to bed, and Mrs. Darling was puzzling over them when Wendy
   312	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsaid with a tolerant smile:
   313	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   314	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I do believe it is that Peter again!”
   315	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   316	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Whatever do you mean, Wendy?”
   317	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   318	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“It is so naughty of him not to wipe his feet,” Wendy said, sighing. She
   319	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas a tidy child.

   395	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

   397	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   398	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   399	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 2 THE SHADOW
   400	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   401	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMrs. Darling screamed, and, as if in answer to a bell, the door opened,
   402	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand Nana entered, returned from her evening out. She growled and sprang
   403	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat the boy, who leapt lightly through the window. Again Mrs. Darling
   404	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tscreamed, this time in distress for him, for she thought he was killed,
   405	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand she ran down into the street to look for his little body, but it
   406	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas not there; and she looked up, and in the black night she could see
   407	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnothing but what she thought was a shooting star.
   408	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

   410	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhich proved to be the boy’s shadow. As he leapt at the window Nana had

   412	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttime to get out; slam went the window and snapped it off.

   415	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tquite the ordinary kind.

   422	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twindow, it looked so like the washing and lowered the whole tone of the

   425	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thead to keep his brain clear, and it seemed a shame to trouble him;
   426	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbesides, she knew exactly what he would say: “It all comes of having a
   427	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdog for a nurse.”

   429	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe decided to roll the shadow up and put it away carefully in a drawer,

   431	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

   433	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOf course it was a Friday.
   434	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

   437	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther, holding her hand.
   438	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

   440	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tGeorge Darling, did it. MEA CULPA, MEA CULPA.” He had had a classical
   441	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\teducation.
   442	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

   444	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdetail of it was stamped on their brains and came through on the other

   448	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsaid.

   451	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDarling.

   468	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit, but Mrs. Darling never upbraided Peter; there was something in the

   504	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   505	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Boy or girl?” asked Michael, not too hopefully.
   506	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

   508	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   509	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThen he had leapt into her arms. Such a little thing for Mr. and Mrs.
   510	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDarling and Nana to recall now, but not so little if that was to be

   512	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
   513	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey go on with their recollections.

   515	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“It was then that I rushed in like a tornado, wasn’t it?” Mr. Darling
   516	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould say, scorning himself; and indeed he had been like a tornado.
   517	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

   519	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe party, and all had gone well with him until he came to his tie. It

   521	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tabout stocks and shares, had no real mastery of his tie. Sometimes the

   617	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\this spirits sank in the strangest way.

  1393	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 4 THE FLIGHT
  1394	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1395	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Second to the right, and straight on till morning.”
  1396	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1398	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbirds, carrying maps and consulting them at windy corners, could not
  1399	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thave sighted it with these instructions. Peter, you see, just said
  1400	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tanything that came into his head.
  1401	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1402	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAt first his companions trusted him implicitly, and so great were the
  1403	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdelights of flying that they wasted time circling round church spires or
  1404	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tany other tall objects on the way that took their fancy.
  1405	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1406	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tJohn and Michael raced, Michael getting a start.
  1407	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1408	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey recalled with contempt that not so long ago they had thought
  1409	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthemselves fine fellows for being able to fly round a room.
  1410	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1413	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsecond sea and their third night.
  1414	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1415	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSometimes it was dark and sometimes light, and now they were very cold
  1416	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand again too warm. Did they really feel hungry at times, or were they
  1417	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmerely pretending, because Peter had such a jolly new way of feeding
  1418	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem? His way was to pursue birds who had food in their mouths suitable
  1419	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfor humans and snatch it from them; then the birds would follow and
  1420	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsnatch it back; and they would all go chasing each other gaily for
  1421	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmiles, parting at last with mutual expressions of good-will. But Wendy

  1423	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas rather an odd way of getting your bread and butter, nor even that
  1424	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthere are other ways.
  1425	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1426	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tCertainly they did not pretend to be sleepy, they were sleepy; and that
  1427	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas a danger, for the moment they popped off, down they fell. The awful
  1428	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthing was that Peter thought this funny.
  1429	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1430	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“There he goes again!” he would cry gleefully, as Michael suddenly
  1431	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdropped like a stone.
  1432	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1434	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsea far below. Eventually Peter would dive through the air, and catch
  1435	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMichael just before he could strike the sea, and it was lovely the way
  1436	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\the did it; but he always waited till the last moment, and you felt it
  1437	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas his cleverness that interested him and not the saving of human life.
  1438	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAlso he was fond of variety, and the sport that engrossed him one moment
  1439	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould suddenly cease to engage him, so there was always the possibility
  1440	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat the next time you fell he would let you go.
  1441	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1442	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe could sleep in the air without falling, by merely lying on his back

  1444	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat if you got behind him and blew he went faster.

  1447	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tplaying “Follow my Leader.”

  1569	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyou that anon fear fell upon them?
  1570	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1571	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt came as the arrows went, leaving the island in gloom.
  1572	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1573	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn the old days at home the Neverland had always begun to look a little
  1574	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdark and threatening by bedtime. Then unexplored patches arose in it
  1575	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand spread, black shadows moved about in them, the roar of the beasts of

  1577	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyou would win. You were quite glad that the night-lights were on. You

  1579	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat the Neverland was all make-believe.
  1580	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1581	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOf course the Neverland had been make-believe in those days, but it
  1582	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas real now, and there were no night-lights, and it was getting darker
  1583	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tevery moment, and where was Nana?

  1585	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey had been flying apart, but they huddled close to Peter now. His
  1586	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcareless manner had gone at last, his eyes were sparkling, and a tingle

  1591	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthrough hostile forces. Sometimes they hung in the air until Peter had
  1592	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbeaten on it with his fists.
  1593	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1595	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1596	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Who are they?” Wendy whispered, shuddering.
  1597	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1598	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBut he could not or would not say. Tinker Bell had been asleep on his
  1599	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshoulder, but now he wakened her and sent her on in front.
  1600	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1601	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSometimes he poised himself in the air, listening intently, with his
  1602	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thand to his ear, and again he would stare down with eyes so bright that
  1603	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey seemed to bore two holes to earth. Having done these things, he
  1604	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twent on again.
  1605	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1606	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHis courage was almost appalling. “Would you like an adventure now,” he
  1607	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsaid casually to John, “or would you like to have your tea first?”
  1608	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1610	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgratitude, but the braver John hesitated.
  1611	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1613	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1615	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“If you like, we’ll go down and kill him.”
  1616	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1618	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1624	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsleeping! I would wake him first, and then kill him. That’s the way I
  1625	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\talways do.”
  1626	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1628	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1630	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1631	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tJohn said “How ripping,” but decided to have tea first. He asked if
  1632	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthere were many pirates on the island just now, and Peter said he had
  1633	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnever known so many.
  1634	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1638	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thated word.

  1641	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1642	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Ay.”
  1643	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1644	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThen indeed Michael began to cry, and even John could speak in gulps
  1645	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tonly, for they knew Hook’s reputation.
  1646	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1648	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof them all. He is the only man of whom Barbecue was afraid.”

  1651	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1653	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1654	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“He is not so big as he was.”
  1655	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1656	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“How do you mean?”
  1657	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1659	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1660	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“You!”
  1661	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1663	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1665	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1669	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1670	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“His right hand.”
  1671	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1682	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I say, John,” said Peter.
  1683	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1685	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1691	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tme has to promise, and so must you.”

  1698	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1699	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFor the moment they were feeling less eerie, because Tink was flying
  1700	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith them, and in her light they could distinguish each other.
  1701	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tUnfortunately she could not fly so slowly as they, and so she had to go
  1702	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tround and round them in a circle in which they moved as in a halo. Wendy
  1703	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tquite liked it, until Peter pointed out the drawbacks.
  1704	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1706	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdarkness came, and got Long Tom out.”
  1707	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1708	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“The big gun?”
  1709	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1710	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes. And of course they must see her light, and if they guess we are
  1711	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnear it they are sure to let fly.”
  1712	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1713	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Wendy!”
  1714	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1715	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“John!”
  1716	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1717	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Michael!”
  1718	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1720	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbut he refused.
  1721	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1723	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trather frightened. You don’t think I would send her away all by herself
  1724	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhen she is frightened!”
  1725	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1726	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFor a moment the circle of light was broken, and something gave Peter a
  1727	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tloving little pinch.
  1728	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  1741	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1742	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHere he got a pinch, but not a loving one.
  1743	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1744	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“If only one of us had a pocket,” Peter said, “we could carry her in
  1745	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit.” However, they had set off in such a hurry that there was not a
  1746	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpocket between the four of them.
  1747	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1748	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe had a happy idea. John’s hat!
  1749	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1750	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTink agreed to travel by hat if it was carried in the hand. John carried
  1751	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit, though she had hoped to be carried by Peter. Presently Wendy took
  1752	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe hat, because John said it struck against his knee as he flew; and
  1753	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthis, as we shall see, led to mischief, for Tinker Bell hated to be
  1754	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tunder an obligation to Wendy.
  1755	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1756	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn the black topper the light was completely hidden, and they flew on in
  1757	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsilence. It was the stillest silence they had ever known, broken once by
  1758	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta distant lapping, which Peter explained was the wild beasts drinking at
  1759	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe ford, and again by a rasping sound that might have been the branches
  1760	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof trees rubbing together, but he said it was the redskins sharpening
  1761	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir knives.
  1762	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1763	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tEven these noises ceased. To Michael the loneliness was dreadful. “If
  1764	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tonly something would make a sound!” he cried.
  1765	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1766	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAs if in answer to his request, the air was rent by the most tremendous

  1768	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1769	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe roar of it echoed through the mountains, and the echoes seemed to
  1770	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcry savagely, “Where are they, where are they, where are they?”
  1771	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1772	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThus sharply did the terrified three learn the difference between an
  1773	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tisland of make-believe and the same island come true.
  1774	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1775	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhen at last the heavens were steady again, John and Michael
  1776	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfound themselves alone in the darkness. John was treading the air
  1777	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmechanically, and Michael without knowing how to float was floating.
  1778	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1779	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Are you shot?” John whispered tremulously.
  1780	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1781	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I haven’t tried [myself out] yet,” Michael whispered back.
  1782	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1783	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWe know now that no one had been hit. Peter, however, had been carried
  1784	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tby the wind of the shot far out to sea, while Wendy was blown upwards
  1785	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith no companion but Tinker Bell.
  1786	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1787	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt would have been well for Wendy if at that moment she had dropped the
  1788	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\that.
  1789	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1790	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tI don’t know whether the idea came suddenly to Tink, or whether she had
  1791	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tplanned it on the way, but she at once popped out of the hat and began
  1792	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto lure Wendy to her destruction.
  1793	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1794	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTink was not all bad; or, rather, she was all bad just now, but, on the
  1795	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tother hand, sometimes she was all good. Fairies have to be one thing or
  1796	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe other, because being so small they unfortunately have room for one
  1797	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfeeling only at a time. They are, however, allowed to change, only it
  1798	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmust be a complete change. At present she was full of jealousy of Wendy.
  1799	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhat she said in her lovely tinkle Wendy could not of course understand,
  1800	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand I believe some of it was bad words, but it sounded kind, and she
  1801	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tflew back and forward, plainly meaning “Follow me, and all will be
  1802	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twell.”
  1803	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1804	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhat else could poor Wendy do? She called to Peter and John and Michael,
  1805	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand got only mocking echoes in reply. She did not yet know that Tink
  1806	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thated her with the fierce hatred of a very woman. And so, bewildered,
  1807	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand now staggering in her flight, she followed Tink to her doom.
  1808	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1809	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1810	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1811	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1812	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 5 THE ISLAND COME TRUE
  1813	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1814	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFeeling that Peter was on his way back, the Neverland had again woke
  1815	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tinto life. We ought to use the pluperfect and say wakened, but woke is
  1816	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbetter and was always used by Peter.
  1817	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1818	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn his absence things are usually quiet on the island. The fairies take
  1819	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tan hour longer in the morning, the beasts attend to their young, the
  1820	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tredskins feed heavily for six days and nights, and when pirates and
  1821	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlost boys meet they merely bite their thumbs at each other. But with the
  1822	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcoming of Peter, who hates lethargy, they are under way again: if you
  1823	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tput your ear to the ground now, you would hear the whole island seething
  1824	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith life.
  1825	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1826	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOn this evening the chief forces of the island were disposed as follows.
  1827	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe lost boys were out looking for Peter, the pirates were out looking
  1828	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfor the lost boys, the redskins were out looking for the pirates, and
  1829	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe beasts were out looking for the redskins. They were going round and
  1830	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tround the island, but they did not meet because all were going at the
  1831	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsame rate.
  1832	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1833	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAll wanted blood except the boys, who liked it as a rule, but to-night
  1834	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twere out to greet their captain. The boys on the island vary, of course,
  1835	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin numbers, according as they get killed and so on; and when they seem
  1836	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto be growing up, which is against the rules, Peter thins them out; but
  1837	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat this time there were six of them, counting the twins as two. Let us
  1838	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpretend to lie here among the sugar-cane and watch them as they steal by
  1839	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin single file, each with his hand on his dagger.
  1840	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1841	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey are forbidden by Peter to look in the least like him, and they wear
  1842	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe skins of the bears slain by themselves, in which they are so round
  1843	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand furry that when they fall they roll. They have therefore become very
  1844	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsure-footed.
  1845	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1846	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe first to pass is Tootles, not the least brave but the most
  1847	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tunfortunate of all that gallant band. He had been in fewer adventures
  1848	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthan any of them, because the big things constantly happened just when
  1849	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\the had stepped round the corner; all would be quiet, he would take the
  1850	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\topportunity of going off to gather a few sticks for firewood, and
  1851	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthen when he returned the others would be sweeping up the blood. This
  1852	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\till-luck had given a gentle melancholy to his countenance, but instead
  1853	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof souring his nature had sweetened it, so that he was quite the
  1854	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thumblest of the boys. Poor kind Tootles, there is danger in the air for
  1855	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyou to-night. Take care lest an adventure is now offered you, which, if
  1856	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\taccepted, will plunge you in deepest woe. Tootles, the fairy Tink, who
  1857	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tis bent on mischief this night is looking for a tool [for doing her
  1858	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmischief], and she thinks you are the most easily tricked of the boys.
  1859	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t‘Ware Tinker Bell.
  1860	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1861	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWould that he could hear us, but we are not really on the island, and he
  1862	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpasses by, biting his knuckles.
  1863	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1864	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNext comes Nibs, the gay and debonair, followed by Slightly, who cuts
  1865	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhistles out of the trees and dances ecstatically to his own tunes.
  1866	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSlightly is the most conceited of the boys. He thinks he remembers the
  1867	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdays before he was lost, with their manners and customs, and this has
  1868	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgiven his nose an offensive tilt. Curly is fourth; he is a pickle, [a
  1869	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tperson who gets in pickles-predicaments] and so often has he had to
  1870	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdeliver up his person when Peter said sternly, “Stand forth the one who
  1871	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdid this thing,” that now at the command he stands forth automatically
  1872	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhether he has done it or not. Last come the Twins, who cannot be
  1873	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdescribed because we should be sure to be describing the wrong one.
  1874	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter never quite knew what twins were, and his band were not allowed
  1875	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto know anything he did not know, so these two were always vague about
  1876	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthemselves, and did their best to give satisfaction by keeping close
  1877	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttogether in an apologetic sort of way.
  1878	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1879	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe boys vanish in the gloom, and after a pause, but not a long pause,
  1880	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfor things go briskly on the island, come the pirates on their track. We
  1881	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thear them before they are seen, and it is always the same dreadful song:
  1882	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1883	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     “Avast belay, yo ho, heave to,
  1884	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     A-pirating we go,
  1885	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     And if we’re parted by a shot
  1886	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     We’re sure to meet below!”
  1887	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1888	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tA more villainous-looking lot never hung in a row on Execution dock.
  1889	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHere, a little in advance, ever and again with his head to the
  1890	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tground listening, his great arms bare, pieces of eight in his ears as
  1891	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tornaments, is the handsome Italian Cecco, who cut his name in letters
  1892	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof blood on the back of the governor of the prison at Gao. That gigantic
  1893	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tblack behind him has had many names since he dropped the one with
  1894	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhich dusky mothers still terrify their children on the banks of the
  1895	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tGuadjo-mo. Here is Bill Jukes, every inch of him tattooed, the same Bill
  1896	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tJukes who got six dozen on the WALRUS from Flint before he would drop
  1897	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe bag of moidores [Portuguese gold pieces]; and Cookson, said to
  1898	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbe Black Murphy’s brother (but this was never proved), and Gentleman
  1899	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tStarkey, once an usher in a public school and still dainty in his ways
  1900	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof killing; and Skylights (Morgan’s Skylights); and the Irish bo’sun
  1901	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSmee, an oddly genial man who stabbed, so to speak, without offence,
  1902	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand was the only Non-conformist in Hook’s crew; and Noodler, whose
  1903	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thands were fixed on backwards; and Robt. Mullins and Alf Mason and many
  1904	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tanother ruffian long known and feared on the Spanish Main.
  1905	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  1906	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn the midst of them, the blackest and largest in that dark setting,
  1907	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\treclined James Hook, or as he wrote himself, Jas. Hook, of whom it is
  1908	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsaid he was the only man that the Sea-Cook feared. He lay at his ease in
  1909	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta rough chariot drawn and propelled by his men, and instead of a right
  1910	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thand he had the iron hook with which ever and anon he encouraged them
  1911	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto increase their pace. As dogs this terrible man treated and addressed
  1912	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem, and as dogs they obeyed him. In person he was cadaverous [dead
  1913	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlooking] and blackavized [dark faced], and his hair was dressed in long
  1914	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcurls, which at a little distance looked like black candles, and gave a
  1915	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsingularly threatening expression to his handsome countenance. His eyes
  1916	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twere of the blue of the forget-me-not, and of a profound melancholy,
  1917	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsave when he was plunging his hook into you, at which time two red spots
  1918	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tappeared in them and lit them up horribly. In manner, something of the

  2223	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2224	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2225	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2226	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2227	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 6 THE LITTLE HOUSE
  2228	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2229	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFoolish Tootles was standing like a conqueror over Wendy’s body when the
  2230	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tother boys sprang, armed, from their trees.
  2231	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2232	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“You are too late,” he cried proudly, “I have shot the Wendy. Peter will
  2233	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbe so pleased with me.”
  2234	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2235	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOverhead Tinker Bell shouted “Silly ass!” and darted into hiding. The
  2236	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tothers did not hear her. They had crowded round Wendy, and as they
  2237	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlooked a terrible silence fell upon the wood. If Wendy’s heart had been
  2238	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbeating they would all have heard it.
  2239	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2240	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSlightly was the first to speak. “This is no bird,” he said in a scared
  2241	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tvoice. “I think this must be a lady.”
  2242	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2243	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“A lady?” said Tootles, and fell a-trembling.
  2244	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2246	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2247	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey all whipped off their caps.
  2248	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2249	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Now I see,” Curly said: “Peter was bringing her to us.” He threw
  2250	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thimself sorrowfully on the ground.
  2251	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2252	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“A lady to take care of us at last,” said one of the twins, “and you
  2253	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thave killed her!”
  2254	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2255	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey were sorry for him, but sorrier for themselves, and when he took a
  2256	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstep nearer them they turned from him.
  2257	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2258	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTootles’ face was very white, but there was a dignity about him now that
  2259	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thad never been there before.
  2260	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2261	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I did it,” he said, reflecting. “When ladies used to come to me in
  2262	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdreams, I said, ‘Pretty mother, pretty mother.’ But when at last she
  2263	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\treally came, I shot her.”
  2264	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2265	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe moved slowly away.
  2266	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2268	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2269	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I must,” he answered, shaking; “I am so afraid of Peter.”
  2270	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2271	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt was at this tragic moment that they heard a sound which made the
  2272	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\theart of every one of them rise to his mouth. They heard Peter crow.
  2273	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2274	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Peter!” they cried, for it was always thus that he signalled his
  2275	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\treturn.
  2276	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2277	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Hide her,” they whispered, and gathered hastily around Wendy. But
  2278	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTootles stood aloof.
  2279	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2280	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAgain came that ringing crow, and Peter dropped in front of them.
  2281	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Greetings, boys,” he cried, and mechanically they saluted, and then
  2282	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tagain was silence.
  2283	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2284	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe frowned.
  2285	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2286	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I am back,” he said hotly, “why do you not cheer?”
  2287	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2288	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey opened their mouths, but the cheers would not come. He overlooked
  2289	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit in his haste to tell the glorious tidings.
  2290	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2291	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Great news, boys,” he cried, “I have brought at last a mother for you
  2292	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tall.”
  2293	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2294	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tStill no sound, except a little thud from Tootles as he dropped on his
  2295	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tknees.
  2296	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2297	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Have you not seen her?” asked Peter, becoming troubled. “She flew this
  2298	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tway.”
  2299	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2300	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Ah me!” one voice said, and another said, “Oh, mournful day.”
  2301	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2303	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhen the others would still have hidden her he said, “Back, twins, let
  2304	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter see.”
  2305	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2306	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSo they all stood back, and let him see, and after he had looked for a
  2307	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlittle time he did not know what to do next.
  2308	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2309	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“She is dead,” he said uncomfortably. “Perhaps she is frightened at
  2310	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbeing dead.”
  2311	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2313	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsight of her, and then never going near the spot any more. They would

  2315	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2317	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2321	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2324	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2327	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2329	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstrike,” he said with awe, “there is something stays my hand.”
  2330	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2331	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAll looked at him in wonder, save Nibs, who fortunately looked at Wendy.
  2332	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2334	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2335	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWonderful to relate [tell], Wendy had raised her arm. Nibs bent over
  2336	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther and listened reverently. “I think she said, ‘Poor Tootles,’” he

  2338	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2340	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2341	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSlightly cried instantly, “The Wendy lady lives.”
  2342	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2343	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThen Peter knelt beside her and found his button. You remember she had

  2345	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2347	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther. It has saved her life.”
  2348	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2350	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat’s a kiss.”
  2351	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2352	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter did not hear him. He was begging Wendy to get better quickly, so
  2353	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat he could show her the mermaids. Of course she could not answer yet,
  2354	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbeing still in a frightful faint; but from overhead came a wailing note.
  2355	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2357	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2358	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThen they had to tell Peter of Tink’s crime, and almost never had they
  2359	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tseen him look so stern.
  2360	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2368	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDo you think Tinker Bell was grateful to Wendy for raising her arm? Oh
  2369	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdear no, never wanted to pinch her so much. Fairies indeed are strange,
  2370	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand Peter, who understood them best, often cuffed [slapped] them.
  2371	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2379	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsufficiently respectful.”

  2382	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2390	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe best of what we have. Gut our house. Be sharp.”
  2391	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2392	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn a moment they were as busy as tailors the night before a wedding.
  2393	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey skurried this way and that, down for bedding, up for firewood, and
  2394	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhile they were at it, who should appear but John and Michael. As they
  2395	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdragged along the ground they fell asleep standing, stopped, woke up,
  2396	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmoved another step and slept again.
  2397	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2399	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmother?”
  2400	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2401	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAnd then John would rub his eyes and mutter, “It is true, we did fly.”
  2402	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2499	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2500	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     “I wish I had a pretty house,
  2501	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     The littlest ever seen,

  2530	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2532	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2533	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     “We’ve made the roses peeping out,
  2534	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     The babes are at the door,
  2535	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     We cannot make ourselves, you know,
  2536	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t     ‘cos we’ve been made before.”
  2537	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2538	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter, seeing this to be a good idea, at once pretended that it was his
  2539	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\town. The house was quite beautiful, and no doubt Wendy was very cosy
  2540	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twithin, though, of course, they could no longer see her. Peter strode

  2548	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2550	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
