  2555	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter an idea. He snatched the hat off John’s head, knocked out the
  2556	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbottom [top], and put the hat on the roof. The little house was so
  2557	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpleased to have such a capital chimney that, as if to say thank you,
  2558	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsmoke immediately began to come out of the hat.
  2559	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2560	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNow really and truly it was finished. Nothing remained to do but to
  2561	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tknock.
  2562	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2563	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“All look your best,” Peter warned them; “first impressions are awfully
  2564	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\timportant.”
  2565	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2566	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe was glad no one asked him what first impressions are; they were all
  2567	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttoo busy looking their best.
  2568	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2569	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe knocked politely, and now the wood was as still as the children, not
  2570	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta sound to be heard except from Tinker Bell, who was watching from a
  2571	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbranch and openly sneering.
  2572	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2573	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhat the boys were wondering was, would any one answer the knock? If a
  2574	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlady, what would she be like?
  2575	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2576	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe door opened and a lady came out. It was Wendy. They all whipped off
  2577	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir hats.
  2578	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2579	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe looked properly surprised, and this was just how they had hoped she
  2580	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould look.
  2581	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2582	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Where am I?” she said.
  2583	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2584	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOf course Slightly was the first to get his word in. “Wendy lady,” he
  2585	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsaid rapidly, “for you we built this house.”
  2586	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2587	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Oh, say you’re pleased,” cried Nibs.
  2588	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2589	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Lovely, darling house,” Wendy said, and they were the very words they
  2590	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thad hoped she would say.
  2591	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2592	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“And we are your children,” cried the twins.
  2593	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2594	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThen all went on their knees, and holding out their arms cried, “O Wendy
  2595	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlady, be our mother.”
  2596	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2597	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Ought I?” Wendy said, all shining. “Of course it’s frightfully
  2598	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfascinating, but you see I am only a little girl. I have no real
  2599	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\texperience.”
  2600	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2601	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“That doesn’t matter,” said Peter, as if he were the only person present
  2602	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twho knew all about it, though he was really the one who knew least.
  2603	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“What we need is just a nice motherly person.”
  2604	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2605	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Oh dear!” Wendy said, “you see, I feel that is exactly what I am.”
  2606	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2607	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“It is, it is,” they all cried; “we saw it at once.”
  2608	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2609	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Very well,” she said, “I will do my best. Come inside at once, you
  2610	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnaughty children; I am sure your feet are damp. And before I put you to
  2611	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbed I have just time to finish the story of Cinderella.”
  2612	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2613	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn they went; I don’t know how there was room for them, but you can
  2614	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsqueeze very tight in the Neverland. And that was the first of the many
  2615	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tjoyous evenings they had with Wendy. By and by she tucked them up in the
  2616	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgreat bed in the home under the trees, but she herself slept that night
  2617	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin the little house, and Peter kept watch outside with drawn sword, for
  2618	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe pirates could be heard carousing far away and the wolves were on the
  2619	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tprowl. The little house looked so cosy and safe in the darkness, with
  2620	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta bright light showing through its blinds, and the chimney smoking
  2621	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbeautifully, and Peter standing on guard. After a time he fell asleep,
  2622	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand some unsteady fairies had to climb over him on their way home from
  2623	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tan orgy. Any of the other boys obstructing the fairy path at night they
  2624	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould have mischiefed, but they just tweaked Peter’s nose and passed on.
  2625	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2626	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2627	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2628	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2629	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 7 THE HOME UNDER THE GROUND
  2630	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2631	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOne of the first things Peter did next day was to measure Wendy and John
  2632	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand Michael for hollow trees. Hook, you remember, had sneered at the
  2633	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tboys for thinking they needed a tree apiece, but this was ignorance, for
  2634	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tunless your tree fitted you it was difficult to go up and down, and no
  2635	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttwo of the boys were quite the same size. Once you fitted, you drew in
  2636	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t[let out] your breath at the top, and down you went at exactly the
  2637	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tright speed, while to ascend you drew in and let out alternately, and so
  2638	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twriggled up. Of course, when you have mastered the action you are able
  2639	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto do these things without thinking of them, and nothing can be more
  2640	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgraceful.
  2641	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2642	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBut you simply must fit, and Peter measures you for your tree as
  2643	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcarefully as for a suit of clothes: the only difference being that the
  2644	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tclothes are made to fit you, while you have to be made to fit the tree.
  2645	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tUsually it is done quite easily, as by your wearing too many garments
  2646	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tor too few, but if you are bumpy in awkward places or the only available
  2647	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttree is an odd shape, Peter does some things to you, and after that you
  2648	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfit. Once you fit, great care must be taken to go on fitting, and this,
  2649	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tas Wendy was to discover to her delight, keeps a whole family in perfect
  2650	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcondition.
  2651	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2652	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy and Michael fitted their trees at the first try, but John had to
  2653	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbe altered a little.
  2654	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2655	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAfter a few days’ practice they could go up and down as gaily as buckets
  2656	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin a well. And how ardently they grew to love their home under the
  2657	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tground; especially Wendy. It consisted of one large room, as all houses
  2658	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshould do, with a floor in which you could dig [for worms] if you wanted
  2659	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto go fishing, and in this floor grew stout mushrooms of a charming
  2660	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcolour, which were used as stools. A Never tree tried hard to grow in
  2661	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe centre of the room, but every morning they sawed the trunk through,
  2662	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlevel with the floor. By tea-time it was always about two feet high, and
  2663	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthen they put a door on top of it, the whole thus becoming a table;
  2664	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tas soon as they cleared away, they sawed off the trunk again, and thus
  2665	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthere was more room to play. There was an enormous fireplace which was
  2666	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin almost any part of the room where you cared to light it, and across
  2667	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthis Wendy stretched strings, made of fibre, from which she suspended
  2668	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther washing. The bed was tilted against the wall by day, and let down at
  2669	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t6:30, when it filled nearly half the room; and all the boys slept in it,
  2670	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\texcept Michael, lying like sardines in a tin. There was a strict rule
  2671	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tagainst turning round until one gave the signal, when all turned at
  2672	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tonce. Michael should have used it also, but Wendy would have [desired]
  2673	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta baby, and he was the littlest, and you know what women are, and the
  2674	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshort and long of it is that he was hung up in a basket.
  2675	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2676	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt was rough and simple, and not unlike what baby bears would have made
  2677	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof an underground house in the same circumstances. But there was one
  2678	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trecess in the wall, no larger than a bird-cage, which was the private
  2679	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tapartment of Tinker Bell. It could be shut off from the rest of
  2680	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe house by a tiny curtain, which Tink, who was most fastidious
  2681	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t[particular], always kept drawn when dressing or undressing. No woman,
  2682	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thowever large, could have had a more exquisite boudoir [dressing room]
  2683	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand bed-chamber combined. The couch, as she always called it, was
  2684	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta genuine Queen Mab, with club legs; and she varied the bedspreads
  2685	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\taccording to what fruit-blossom was in season. Her mirror was a
  2686	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPuss-in-Boots, of which there are now only three, unchipped, known to
  2687	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfairy dealers; the washstand was Pie-crust and reversible, the chest
  2688	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof drawers an authentic Charming the Sixth, and the carpet and rugs the
  2689	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbest (the early) period of Margery and Robin. There was a chandelier
  2690	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfrom Tiddlywinks for the look of the thing, but of course she lit the
  2691	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tresidence herself. Tink was very contemptuous of the rest of the house,
  2692	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tas indeed was perhaps inevitable, and her chamber, though beautiful,
  2693	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlooked rather conceited, having the appearance of a nose permanently
  2694	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tturned up.
  2695	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2696	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tI suppose it was all especially entrancing to Wendy, because those
  2697	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trampagious boys of hers gave her so much to do. Really there were whole
  2698	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tweeks when, except perhaps with a stocking in the evening, she was never
  2699	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tabove ground. The cooking, I can tell you, kept her nose to the pot, and
  2700	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\teven if there was nothing in it, even if there was no pot, she had to
  2701	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tkeep watching that it came aboil just the same. You never exactly
  2702	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tknew whether there would be a real meal or just a make-believe, it all
  2703	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdepended upon Peter’s whim: he could eat, really eat, if it was part of
  2704	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta game, but he could not stodge [cram down the food] just to feel
  2705	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstodgy [stuffed with food], which is what most children like better than
  2706	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tanything else; the next best thing being to talk about it. Make-believe
  2707	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas so real to him that during a meal of it you could see him getting
  2708	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trounder. Of course it was trying, but you simply had to follow his lead,
  2709	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand if you could prove to him that you were getting loose for your tree
  2710	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\the let you stodge.
  2711	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2712	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy’s favourite time for sewing and darning was after they had all
  2713	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgone to bed. Then, as she expressed it, she had a breathing time for
  2714	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\therself; and she occupied it in making new things for them, and putting
  2715	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdouble pieces on the knees, for they were all most frightfully hard on
  2716	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir knees.
  2717	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2718	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhen she sat down to a basketful of their stockings, every heel with a
  2719	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thole in it, she would fling up her arms and exclaim, “Oh dear, I am sure
  2720	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tI sometimes think spinsters are to be envied!”
  2721	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2722	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHer face beamed when she exclaimed this.
  2723	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2724	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tYou remember about her pet wolf. Well, it very soon discovered that she
  2725	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thad come to the island and it found her out, and they just ran into each
  2726	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tother’s arms. After that it followed her about everywhere.
  2727	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2728	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAs time wore on did she think much about the beloved parents she had
  2729	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tleft behind her? This is a difficult question, because it is quite
  2730	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\timpossible to say how time does wear on in the Neverland, where it is
  2731	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcalculated by moons and suns, and there are ever so many more of them
  2732	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthan on the mainland. But I am afraid that Wendy did not really worry
  2733	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tabout her father and mother; she was absolutely confident that they
  2734	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould always keep the window open for her to fly back by, and this gave
  2735	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther complete ease of mind. What did disturb her at times was that John
  2736	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tremembered his parents vaguely only, as people he had once known, while
  2737	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMichael was quite willing to believe that she was really his mother.
  2738	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThese things scared her a little, and nobly anxious to do her duty, she
  2739	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttried to fix the old life in their minds by setting them examination
  2740	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpapers on it, as like as possible to the ones she used to do at school.
  2741	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe other boys thought this awfully interesting, and insisted on
  2742	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tjoining, and they made slates for themselves, and sat round the table,
  2743	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twriting and thinking hard about the questions she had written on another
  2744	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tslate and passed round. They were the most ordinary questions--“What
  2745	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas the colour of Mother’s eyes? Which was taller, Father or Mother? Was
  2746	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMother blonde or brunette? Answer all three questions if possible.”
  2747	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t “(A) Write an essay of not less than 40 words on How I spent my last
  2748	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHolidays, or The Characters of Father and Mother compared. Only one of
  2749	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthese to be attempted.” Or “(1) Describe Mother’s laugh; (2) Describe
  2750	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFather’s laugh; (3) Describe Mother’s Party Dress; (4) Describe the
  2751	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tKennel and its Inmate.”
  2752	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2753	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey were just everyday questions like these, and when you could not
  2754	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tanswer them you were told to make a cross; and it was really dreadful
  2755	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhat a number of crosses even John made. Of course the only boy who
  2756	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\treplied to every question was Slightly, and no one could have been more
  2757	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thopeful of coming out first, but his answers were perfectly ridiculous,
  2758	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand he really came out last: a melancholy thing.
  2759	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2760	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter did not compete. For one thing he despised all mothers except
  2761	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy, and for another he was the only boy on the island who could
  2762	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tneither write nor spell; not the smallest word. He was above all that
  2763	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsort of thing.
  2764	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2765	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBy the way, the questions were all written in the past tense. What
  2766	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas the colour of Mother’s eyes, and so on. Wendy, you see, had been
  2767	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tforgetting, too.
  2768	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2769	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAdventures, of course, as we shall see, were of daily occurrence; but
  2770	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tabout this time Peter invented, with Wendy’s help, a new game that
  2771	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfascinated him enormously, until he suddenly had no more interest in it,
  2772	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhich, as you have been told, was what always happened with his games.
  2773	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt consisted in pretending not to have adventures, in doing the sort of
  2774	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthing John and Michael had been doing all their lives, sitting on stools
  2775	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tflinging balls in the air, pushing each other, going out for walks and
  2776	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcoming back without having killed so much as a grizzly. To see Peter
  2777	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdoing nothing on a stool was a great sight; he could not help looking
  2778	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsolemn at such times, to sit still seemed to him such a comic thing to
  2779	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdo. He boasted that he had gone walking for the good of his health. For
  2780	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tseveral suns these were the most novel of all adventures to him; and
  2781	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tJohn and Michael had to pretend to be delighted also; otherwise he would
  2782	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thave treated them severely.
  2783	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2784	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe often went out alone, and when he came back you were never absolutely
  2785	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcertain whether he had had an adventure or not. He might have forgotten
  2786	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit so completely that he said nothing about it; and then when you went
  2787	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tout you found the body; and, on the other hand, he might say a great
  2788	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdeal about it, and yet you could not find the body. Sometimes he came
  2789	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thome with his head bandaged, and then Wendy cooed over him and bathed
  2790	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit in lukewarm water, while he told a dazzling tale. But she was never
  2791	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tquite sure, you know. There were, however, many adventures which she
  2792	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tknew to be true because she was in them herself, and there were still
  2793	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmore that were at least partly true, for the other boys were in them and
  2794	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsaid they were wholly true. To describe them all would require a book as
  2795	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlarge as an English-Latin, Latin-English Dictionary, and the most we can
  2796	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdo is to give one as a specimen of an average hour on the island. The
  2797	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdifficulty is which one to choose. Should we take the brush with the
  2798	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tredskins at Slightly Gulch? It was a sanguinary affair, and
  2799	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tespecially interesting as showing one of Peter’s peculiarities, which
  2800	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas that in the middle of a fight he would suddenly change sides. At the
  2801	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tGulch, when victory was still in the balance, sometimes leaning this way
  2802	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand sometimes that, he called out, “I’m redskin to-day; what are you,
  2803	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTootles?” And Tootles answered, “Redskin; what are you, Nibs?” and
  2804	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNibs said, “Redskin; what are you Twin?” and so on; and they were all
  2805	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tredskins; and of course this would have ended the fight had not the real
  2806	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tredskins fascinated by Peter’s methods, agreed to be lost boys for that
  2807	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tonce, and so at it they all went again, more fiercely than ever.
  2808	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2809	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe extraordinary upshot of this adventure was--but we have not decided
  2810	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyet that this is the adventure we are to narrate. Perhaps a better one
  2811	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould be the night attack by the redskins on the house under the ground,
  2812	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhen several of them stuck in the hollow trees and had to be pulled out
  2813	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlike corks. Or we might tell how Peter saved Tiger Lily’s life in the
  2814	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMermaids’ Lagoon, and so made her his ally.
  2815	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2816	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOr we could tell of that cake the pirates cooked so that the boys might
  2817	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\teat it and perish; and how they placed it in one cunning spot after
  2818	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tanother; but always Wendy snatched it from the hands of her children, so
  2819	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat in time it lost its succulence, and became as hard as a stone, and
  2820	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas used as a missile, and Hook fell over it in the dark.
  2821	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2822	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOr suppose we tell of the birds that were Peter’s friends, particularly
  2823	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof the Never bird that built in a tree overhanging the lagoon, and how
  2824	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe nest fell into the water, and still the bird sat on her eggs, and
  2825	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter gave orders that she was not to be disturbed. That is a pretty
  2826	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstory, and the end shows how grateful a bird can be; but if we tell
  2827	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit we must also tell the whole adventure of the lagoon, which would
  2828	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof course be telling two adventures rather than just one. A shorter
  2829	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tadventure, and quite as exciting, was Tinker Bell’s attempt, with the
  2830	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thelp of some street fairies, to have the sleeping Wendy conveyed on a
  2831	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgreat floating leaf to the mainland. Fortunately the leaf gave way and
  2832	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy woke, thinking it was bath-time, and swam back. Or again, we might
  2833	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tchoose Peter’s defiance of the lions, when he drew a circle round him
  2834	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ton the ground with an arrow and dared them to cross it; and though he
  2835	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twaited for hours, with the other boys and Wendy looking on breathlessly
  2836	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfrom trees, not one of them dared to accept his challenge.
  2837	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2838	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhich of these adventures shall we choose? The best way will be to toss
  2839	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfor it.
  2840	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2841	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tI have tossed, and the lagoon has won. This almost makes one wish that
  2842	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe gulch or the cake or Tink’s leaf had won. Of course I could do it
  2843	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tagain, and make it best out of three; however, perhaps fairest to stick
  2844	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto the lagoon.
  2845	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2846	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2847	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2848	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2849	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 8 THE MERMAIDS’ LAGOON
  2850	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2851	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIf you shut your eyes and are a lucky one, you may see at times a
  2852	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshapeless pool of lovely pale colours suspended in the darkness; then
  2853	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tif you squeeze your eyes tighter, the pool begins to take shape, and the
  2854	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcolours become so vivid that with another squeeze they must go on fire.
  2855	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBut just before they go on fire you see the lagoon. This is the nearest
  2856	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyou ever get to it on the mainland, just one heavenly moment; if there
  2857	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcould be two moments you might see the surf and hear the mermaids
  2858	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsinging.
  2859	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2860	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe children often spent long summer days on this lagoon, swimming or
  2861	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfloating most of the time, playing the mermaid games in the water,
  2862	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand so forth. You must not think from this that the mermaids were on
  2863	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfriendly terms with them: on the contrary, it was among Wendy’s lasting
  2864	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tregrets that all the time she was on the island she never had a civil
  2865	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tword from one of them. When she stole softly to the edge of the lagoon
  2866	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe might see them by the score, especially on Marooners’ Rock, where
  2867	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey loved to bask, combing out their hair in a lazy way that quite
  2868	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tirritated her; or she might even swim, on tiptoe as it were, to within
  2869	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta yard of them, but then they saw her and dived, probably splashing her
  2870	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith their tails, not by accident, but intentionally.
  2871	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2872	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey treated all the boys in the same way, except of course Peter, who
  2873	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tchatted with them on Marooners’ Rock by the hour, and sat on their tails
  2874	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhen they got cheeky. He gave Wendy one of their combs.
  2875	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2876	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe most haunting time at which to see them is at the turn of the moon,
  2877	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhen they utter strange wailing cries; but the lagoon is dangerous for
  2878	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmortals then, and until the evening of which we have now to tell, Wendy
  2879	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thad never seen the lagoon by moonlight, less from fear, for of course
  2880	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter would have accompanied her, than because she had strict rules
  2881	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tabout every one being in bed by seven. She was often at the lagoon,
  2882	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thowever, on sunny days after rain, when the mermaids come up in
  2883	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\textraordinary numbers to play with their bubbles. The bubbles of many
  2884	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcolours made in rainbow water they treat as balls, hitting them gaily
  2885	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfrom one to another with their tails, and trying to keep them in the
  2886	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trainbow till they burst. The goals are at each end of the rainbow, and
  2887	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe keepers only are allowed to use their hands. Sometimes a dozen of
  2888	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthese games will be going on in the lagoon at a time, and it is quite a
  2889	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpretty sight.
  2890	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2891	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBut the moment the children tried to join in they had to play by
  2892	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthemselves, for the mermaids immediately disappeared. Nevertheless we
  2893	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thave proof that they secretly watched the interlopers, and were not
  2894	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tabove taking an idea from them; for John introduced a new way of hitting
  2895	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe bubble, with the head instead of the hand, and the mermaids adopted
  2896	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit. This is the one mark that John has left on the Neverland.
  2897	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2898	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt must also have been rather pretty to see the children resting on a
  2899	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trock for half an hour after their mid-day meal. Wendy insisted on
  2900	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir doing this, and it had to be a real rest even though the meal was
  2901	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmake-believe. So they lay there in the sun, and their bodies glistened
  2902	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin it, while she sat beside them and looked important.
  2903	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2904	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt was one such day, and they were all on Marooners’ Rock. The rock was
  2905	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnot much larger than their great bed, but of course they all knew how
  2906	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnot to take up much room, and they were dozing, or at least lying with
  2907	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir eyes shut, and pinching occasionally when they thought Wendy was
  2908	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnot looking. She was very busy, stitching.
  2909	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2910	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhile she stitched a change came to the lagoon. Little shivers ran over
  2911	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit, and the sun went away and shadows stole across the water, turning
  2912	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit cold. Wendy could no longer see to thread her needle, and when she
  2913	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlooked up, the lagoon that had always hitherto been such a laughing
  2914	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tplace seemed formidable and unfriendly.
  2915	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2916	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt was not, she knew, that night had come, but something as dark as
  2917	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnight had come. No, worse than that. It had not come, but it had sent
  2918	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat shiver through the sea to say that it was coming. What was it?
  2919	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2920	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThere crowded upon her all the stories she had been told of Marooners’
  2921	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tRock, so called because evil captains put sailors on it and leave
  2922	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem there to drown. They drown when the tide rises, for then it is
  2923	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsubmerged.
  2924	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2925	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOf course she should have roused the children at once; not merely
  2926	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbecause of the unknown that was stalking toward them, but because it was
  2927	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tno longer good for them to sleep on a rock grown chilly. But she was
  2928	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta young mother and she did not know this; she thought you simply must
  2929	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstick to your rule about half an hour after the mid-day meal. So, though
  2930	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfear was upon her, and she longed to hear male voices, she would not
  2931	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twaken them. Even when she heard the sound of muffled oars, though her
  2932	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\theart was in her mouth, she did not waken them. She stood over them to
  2933	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlet them have their sleep out. Was it not brave of Wendy?
  2934	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2935	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt was well for those boys then that there was one among them who could
  2936	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsniff danger even in his sleep. Peter sprang erect, as wide awake at
  2937	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tonce as a dog, and with one warning cry he roused the others.
  2938	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2939	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe stood motionless, one hand to his ear.
  2940	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2941	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Pirates!” he cried. The others came closer to him. A strange smile was
  2942	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tplaying about his face, and Wendy saw it and shuddered. While that smile
  2943	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas on his face no one dared address him; all they could do was to stand
  2944	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tready to obey. The order came sharp and incisive.
  2945	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2946	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Dive!”
  2947	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2948	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThere was a gleam of legs, and instantly the lagoon seemed deserted.
  2949	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMarooners’ Rock stood alone in the forbidding waters as if it were
  2950	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\titself marooned.
  2951	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2952	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe boat drew nearer. It was the pirate dinghy, with three figures in
  2953	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther, Smee and Starkey, and the third a captive, no other than Tiger
  2954	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tLily. Her hands and ankles were tied, and she knew what was to be her
  2955	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfate. She was to be left on the rock to perish, an end to one of her
  2956	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trace more terrible than death by fire or torture, for is it not written
  2957	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin the book of the tribe that there is no path through water to the
  2958	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thappy hunting-ground? Yet her face was impassive; she was the daughter
  2959	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof a chief, she must die as a chief’s daughter, it is enough.
  2960	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2961	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey had caught her boarding the pirate ship with a knife in her mouth.
  2962	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNo watch was kept on the ship, it being Hook’s boast that the wind of
  2963	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\this name guarded the ship for a mile around. Now her fate would help to
  2964	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tguard it also. One more wail would go the round in that wind by night.
  2965	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2966	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn the gloom that they brought with them the two pirates did not see the
  2967	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trock till they crashed into it.
  2968	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2969	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Luff, you lubber,” cried an Irish voice that was Smee’s; “here’s the
  2970	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trock. Now, then, what we have to do is to hoist the redskin on to it and
  2971	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tleave her here to drown.”
  2972	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2973	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt was the work of one brutal moment to land the beautiful girl on the
  2974	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trock; she was too proud to offer a vain resistance.
  2975	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2976	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tQuite near the rock, but out of sight, two heads were bobbing up and
  2977	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdown, Peter’s and Wendy’s. Wendy was crying, for it was the first
  2978	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttragedy she had seen. Peter had seen many tragedies, but he had
  2979	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tforgotten them all. He was less sorry than Wendy for Tiger Lily: it was
  2980	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttwo against one that angered him, and he meant to save her. An easy way
  2981	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twould have been to wait until the pirates had gone, but he was never one
  2982	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto choose the easy way.
  2983	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2984	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThere was almost nothing he could not do, and he now imitated the voice
  2985	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof Hook.
  2986	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2987	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Ahoy there, you lubbers!” he called. It was a marvellous imitation.
  2988	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2989	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“The captain!” said the pirates, staring at each other in surprise.
  2990	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2991	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“He must be swimming out to us,” Starkey said, when they had looked for
  2992	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thim in vain.
  2993	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2995	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  2997	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  2998	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Free!”
  2999	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3000	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes, cut her bonds and let her go.”
  3001	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3002	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“But, captain--”

  3011	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe slid between Starkey’s legs into the water.
  3012	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  3014	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat he would be elated also and very likely crow and thus betray

  3283	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt would not have been fighting fair. He gave the pirate a hand to help
  3284	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thim up.

  3288	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNot the pain of this but its unfairness was what dazed Peter. It made
  3289	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thim quite helpless. He could only stare, horrified. Every child is
  3290	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\taffected thus the first time he is treated unfairly. All he thinks he

  3295	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit. I suppose that was the real difference between him and all the rest.

  3307	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsave mocking laughter from the mermaids. “They must be swimming back or

  3395	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3396	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 9 THE NEVER BIRD
  3397	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3398	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe last sound Peter heard before he was quite alone were the mermaids
  3399	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tretiring one by one to their bedchambers under the sea. He was too far
  3400	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\taway to hear their doors shut; but every door in the coral caves where
  3401	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey live rings a tiny bell when it opens or closes (as in all the
  3402	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnicest houses on the mainland), and he heard the bells.
  3403	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3404	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSteadily the waters rose till they were nibbling at his feet; and to
  3405	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpass the time until they made their final gulp, he watched the only
  3406	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthing on the lagoon. He thought it was a piece of floating paper,
  3407	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tperhaps part of the kite, and wondered idly how long it would take to
  3408	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdrift ashore.
  3409	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3410	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPresently he noticed as an odd thing that it was undoubtedly out upon
  3411	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe lagoon with some definite purpose, for it was fighting the tide,
  3412	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand sometimes winning; and when it won, Peter, always sympathetic to
  3413	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe weaker side, could not help clapping; it was such a gallant piece of
  3414	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpaper.
  3415	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3416	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt was not really a piece of paper; it was the Never bird, making
  3417	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdesperate efforts to reach Peter on the nest. By working her wings, in a
  3418	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tway she had learned since the nest fell into the water, she was able to
  3419	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsome extent to guide her strange craft, but by the time Peter recognised
  3420	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther she was very exhausted. She had come to save him, to give him her
  3421	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnest, though there were eggs in it. I rather wonder at the bird, for
  3422	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthough he had been nice to her, he had also sometimes tormented her. I
  3423	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcan suppose only that, like Mrs. Darling and the rest of them, she was
  3424	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmelted because he had all his first teeth.
  3425	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3426	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe called out to him what she had come for, and he called out to her
  3427	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhat she was doing there; but of course neither of them understood
  3428	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe other’s language. In fanciful stories people can talk to the birds
  3429	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfreely, and I wish for the moment I could pretend that this were such a
  3430	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstory, and say that Peter replied intelligently to the Never bird; but
  3431	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttruth is best, and I want to tell you only what really happened. Well,
  3432	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnot only could they not understand each other, but they forgot their
  3433	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmanners.
  3434	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3435	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I--want--you--to--get--into--the--nest,” the bird called, speaking as
  3436	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tslowly and distinctly as possible, “and--then--you--can--drift--ashore,
  3437	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbut--I--am--too--tired--to--bring--it--any--nearer--so--you--must--try
  3438	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto--swim--to--it.”
  3439	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3440	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“What are you quacking about?” Peter answered. “Why don’t you let the
  3441	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnest drift as usual?”
  3442	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3443	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I--want--you--” the bird said, and repeated it all over.
  3444	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3445	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThen Peter tried slow and distinct.
  3446	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3447	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“What--are--you--quacking--about?” and so on.
  3448	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3449	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe Never bird became irritated; they have very short tempers.
  3450	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3451	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“You dunderheaded little jay!” she screamed, “Why don’t you do as I tell
  3452	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyou?”
  3453	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3454	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter felt that she was calling him names, and at a venture he retorted
  3455	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thotly:
  3456	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3457	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“So are you!”
  3458	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3459	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThen rather curiously they both snapped out the same remark:
  3460	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3461	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Shut up!”
  3462	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3463	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Shut up!”
  3464	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3465	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNevertheless the bird was determined to save him if she could, and by
  3466	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tone last mighty effort she propelled the nest against the rock. Then up
  3467	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe flew; deserting her eggs, so as to make her meaning clear.
  3468	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3469	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThen at last he understood, and clutched the nest and waved his thanks
  3470	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto the bird as she fluttered overhead. It was not to receive his thanks,
  3471	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thowever, that she hung there in the sky; it was not even to watch him
  3472	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tget into the nest; it was to see what he did with her eggs.
  3473	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3474	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThere were two large white eggs, and Peter lifted them up and reflected.
  3475	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe bird covered her face with her wings, so as not to see the last of
  3476	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem; but she could not help peeping between the feathers.
  3477	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3478	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tI forget whether I have told you that there was a stave on the rock,
  3479	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdriven into it by some buccaneers of long ago to mark the site of buried
  3480	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttreasure. The children had discovered the glittering hoard, and when in
  3481	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta mischievous mood used to fling showers of moidores, diamonds, pearls
  3482	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand pieces of eight to the gulls, who pounced upon them for food, and
  3483	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthen flew away, raging at the scurvy trick that had been played upon
  3484	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem. The stave was still there, and on it Starkey had hung his hat, a
  3485	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdeep tarpaulin, watertight, with a broad brim. Peter put the eggs into
  3486	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthis hat and set it on the lagoon. It floated beautifully.
  3487	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3488	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe Never bird saw at once what he was up to, and screamed her
  3489	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tadmiration of him; and, alas, Peter crowed his agreement with her. Then
  3490	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\the got into the nest, reared the stave in it as a mast, and hung up his
  3491	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshirt for a sail. At the same moment the bird fluttered down upon the
  3492	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\that and once more sat snugly on her eggs. She drifted in one direction,
  3493	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand he was borne off in another, both cheering.
  3494	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3495	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOf course when Peter landed he beached his barque [small ship, actually
  3496	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe Never Bird’s nest in this particular case in point] in a place where
  3497	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe bird would easily find it; but the hat was such a great success that
  3498	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe abandoned the nest. It drifted about till it went to pieces, and
  3499	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\toften Starkey came to the shore of the lagoon, and with many bitter
  3500	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfeelings watched the bird sitting on his hat. As we shall not see her
  3501	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tagain, it may be worth mentioning here that all Never birds now build
  3502	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin that shape of nest, with a broad brim on which the youngsters take an
  3503	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tairing.
  3504	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3505	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tGreat were the rejoicings when Peter reached the home under the ground
  3506	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\talmost as soon as Wendy, who had been carried hither and thither by
  3507	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe kite. Every boy had adventures to tell; but perhaps the biggest
  3508	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tadventure of all was that they were several hours late for bed. This so
  3509	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tinflated them that they did various dodgy things to get staying up still
  3510	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlonger, such as demanding bandages; but Wendy, though glorying in having
  3511	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem all home again safe and sound, was scandalised by the lateness of
  3512	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe hour, and cried, “To bed, to bed,” in a voice that had to be obeyed.
  3513	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNext day, however, she was awfully tender, and gave out bandages to
  3514	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tevery one, and they played till bed-time at limping about and carrying
  3515	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir arms in slings.
  3516	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3517	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3518	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3519	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3520	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 10 THE HAPPY HOME
  3521	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3522	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOne important result of the brush [with the pirates] on the lagoon was
  3523	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat it made the redskins their friends. Peter had saved Tiger Lily from
  3524	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta dreadful fate, and now there was nothing she and her braves would not
  3525	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdo for him. All night they sat above, keeping watch over the home under
  3526	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe ground and awaiting the big attack by the pirates which obviously
  3527	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcould not be much longer delayed. Even by day they hung about, smoking
  3528	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe pipe of peace, and looking almost as if they wanted tit-bits to eat.
  3529	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3530	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey called Peter the Great White Father, prostrating themselves [lying
  3531	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdown] before him; and he liked this tremendously, so that it was not
  3532	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\treally good for him.
  3533	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3534	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“The great white father,” he would say to them in a very lordly manner,
  3535	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tas they grovelled at his feet, “is glad to see the Piccaninny warriors
  3536	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tprotecting his wigwam from the pirates.”
  3537	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3538	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Me Tiger Lily,” that lovely creature would reply. “Peter Pan save me,
  3539	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tme his velly nice friend. Me no let pirates hurt him.”
  3540	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3541	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe was far too pretty to cringe in this way, but Peter thought it his
  3542	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdue, and he would answer condescendingly, “It is good. Peter Pan has
  3543	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tspoken.”
  3544	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3545	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAlways when he said, “Peter Pan has spoken,” it meant that they must now
  3546	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshut up, and they accepted it humbly in that spirit; but they were by
  3547	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tno means so respectful to the other boys, whom they looked upon as just
  3548	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tordinary braves. They said “How-do?” to them, and things like that; and
  3549	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhat annoyed the boys was that Peter seemed to think this all right.
  3550	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3551	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSecretly Wendy sympathised with them a little, but she was far too loyal
  3552	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta housewife to listen to any complaints against father. “Father knows
  3553	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbest,” she always said, whatever her private opinion must be. Her
  3554	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tprivate opinion was that the redskins should not call her a squaw.
  3555	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3556	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWe have now reached the evening that was to be known among them as the
  3557	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNight of Nights, because of its adventures and their upshot. The day, as
  3558	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tif quietly gathering its forces, had been almost uneventful, and now the
  3559	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tredskins in their blankets were at their posts above, while, below, the
  3560	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tchildren were having their evening meal; all except Peter, who had gone
  3561	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tout to get the time. The way you got the time on the island was to find
  3562	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe crocodile, and then stay near him till the clock struck.
  3563	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  3564	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe meal happened to be a make-believe tea, and they sat around the
  3565	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tboard, guzzling in their greed; and really, what with their chatter and
  3566	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trecriminations, the noise, as Wendy said, was positively deafening.
  3567	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTo be sure, she did not mind noise, but she simply would not have them
  3568	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgrabbing things, and then excusing themselves by saying that Tootles had
  3569	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpushed their elbow. There was a fixed rule that they must never hit back
  3570	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat meals, but should refer the matter of dispute to Wendy by raising
  3571	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe right arm politely and saying, “I complain of so-and-so;” but what
  3572	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tusually happened was that they forgot to do this or did it too much.

  3797	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsang and danced in their night-gowns. Such a deliciously creepy song
  3798	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit was, in which they pretended to be frightened at their own shadows,
  3799	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlittle witting that so soon shadows would close in upon them, from whom
  3800	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey would shrink in real fear. So uproariously gay was the dance, and
  3801	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thow they buffeted each other on the bed and out of it! It was a pillow
  3802	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfight rather than a dance, and when it was finished, the pillows
  3803	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tinsisted on one bout more, like partners who know that they may never
  3804	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmeet again. The stories they told, before it was time for Wendy’s
  3805	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgood-night story! Even Slightly tried to tell a story that night, but
  3806	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe beginning was so fearfully dull that it appalled not only the others
  3807	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbut himself, and he said happily:
  3808	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  3810	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  3815	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe island. But to-night he remained on his stool; and we shall see what
  3816	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thappened.

  4121	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Get your things, Peter,” she cried, shaking.
  4122	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4123	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“No,” he answered, pretending indifference, “I am not going with you,
  4124	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy.”
  4125	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4126	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes, Peter.”
  4127	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4128	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“No.”
  4129	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4130	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTo show that her departure would leave him unmoved, he skipped up and
  4131	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdown the room, playing gaily on his heartless pipes. She had to run
  4132	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tabout after him, though it was rather undignified.
  4133	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4134	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“To find your mother,” she coaxed.
  4135	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4136	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNow, if Peter had ever quite had a mother, he no longer missed her. He
  4137	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcould do very well without one. He had thought them out, and remembered
  4138	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tonly their bad points.
  4139	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4140	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“No, no,” he told Wendy decisively; “perhaps she would say I was old,
  4141	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand I just want always to be a little boy and to have fun.”
  4142	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4143	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“But, Peter--”
  4144	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4145	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“No.”
  4146	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4147	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAnd so the others had to be told.
  4148	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4149	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Peter isn’t coming.”
  4150	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4151	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter not coming! They gazed blankly at him, their sticks over their
  4152	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbacks, and on each stick a bundle. Their first thought was that if Peter
  4153	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas not going he had probably changed his mind about letting them go.
  4154	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4155	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBut he was far too proud for that. “If you find your mothers,” he said
  4156	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdarkly, “I hope you will like them.”
  4157	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4158	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe awful cynicism of this made an uncomfortable impression, and most
  4159	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof them began to look rather doubtful. After all, their faces said, were
  4160	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey not noodles to want to go?
  4161	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4162	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Now then,” cried Peter, “no fuss, no blubbering; good-bye, Wendy;” and
  4163	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\the held out his hand cheerily, quite as if they must really go now, for
  4164	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\the had something important to do.
  4165	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4166	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe had to take his hand, and there was no indication that he would
  4167	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tprefer a thimble.
  4168	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4169	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“You will remember about changing your flannels, Peter?” she said,
  4170	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlingering over him. She was always so particular about their flannels.
  4171	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4172	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes.”
  4173	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4174	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“And you will take your medicine?”
  4175	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4176	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes.”
  4177	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4178	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThat seemed to be everything, and an awkward pause followed. Peter,
  4179	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thowever, was not the kind that breaks down before other people. “Are you
  4180	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tready, Tinker Bell?” he called out.
  4181	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  4183	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4184	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Then lead the way.”
  4185	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4186	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTink darted up the nearest tree; but no one followed her, for it was
  4187	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat this moment that the pirates made their dreadful attack upon the
  4188	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tredskins. Above, where all had been so still, the air was rent with

  4190	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\topened and remained open. Wendy fell on her knees, but her arms were

  4192	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tblown in his direction; they were beseeching him mutely not to desert
  4193	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem. As for Peter, he seized his sword, the same he thought he had
  4194	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tslain Barbecue with, and the lust of battle was in his eye.
  4195	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4196	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4197	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4198	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4199	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 12 THE CHILDREN ARE CARRIED OFF
  4200	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4201	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe pirate attack had been a complete surprise: a sure proof that the
  4202	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tunscrupulous Hook had conducted it improperly, for to surprise redskins
  4203	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfairly is beyond the wit of the white man.
  4204	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4205	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBy all the unwritten laws of savage warfare it is always the redskin who
  4206	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tattacks, and with the wiliness of his race he does it just before the
  4207	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdawn, at which time he knows the courage of the whites to be at its
  4208	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlowest ebb. The white men have in the meantime made a rude stockade on
  4209	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe summit of yonder undulating ground, at the foot of which a stream
  4210	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\truns, for it is destruction to be too far from water. There they await
  4211	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe onslaught, the inexperienced ones clutching their revolvers and
  4212	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttreading on twigs, but the old hands sleeping tranquilly until just
  4213	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbefore the dawn. Through the long black night the savage scouts wriggle,
  4214	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsnake-like, among the grass without stirring a blade. The brushwood
  4215	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcloses behind them, as silently as sand into which a mole has dived.
  4216	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNot a sound is to be heard, save when they give vent to a wonderful
  4217	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\timitation of the lonely call of the coyote. The cry is answered by other
  4218	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbraves; and some of them do it even better than the coyotes, who are not
  4219	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tvery good at it. So the chill hours wear on, and the long suspense is
  4220	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thorribly trying to the paleface who has to live through it for the first
  4221	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttime; but to the trained hand those ghastly calls and still ghastlier
  4222	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsilences are but an intimation of how the night is marching.
  4223	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4224	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThat this was the usual procedure was so well known to Hook that in
  4225	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdisregarding it he cannot be excused on the plea of ignorance.
  4226	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4227	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe Piccaninnies, on their part, trusted implicitly to his honour, and
  4228	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir whole action of the night stands out in marked contrast to his.
  4229	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey left nothing undone that was consistent with the reputation of
  4230	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir tribe. With that alertness of the senses which is at once the
  4231	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmarvel and despair of civilised peoples, they knew that the pirates were
  4232	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ton the island from the moment one of them trod on a dry stick; and in
  4233	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tan incredibly short space of time the coyote cries began. Every foot of
  4234	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tground between the spot where Hook had landed his forces and the
  4235	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thome under the trees was stealthily examined by braves wearing their
  4236	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmocassins with the heels in front. They found only one hillock with a
  4237	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstream at its base, so that Hook had no choice; here he must establish
  4238	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thimself and wait for just before the dawn. Everything being thus mapped
  4239	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tout with almost diabolical cunning, the main body of the redskins folded
  4240	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir blankets around them, and in the phlegmatic manner that is to
  4241	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem, the pearl of manhood squatted above the children’s home, awaiting
  4242	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe cold moment when they should deal pale death.
  4243	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4244	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHere dreaming, though wide-awake, of the exquisite tortures to which
  4245	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey were to put him at break of day, those confiding savages were found
  4246	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tby the treacherous Hook. From the accounts afterwards supplied by such
  4247	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof the scouts as escaped the carnage, he does not seem even to have
  4248	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpaused at the rising ground, though it is certain that in that grey
  4249	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlight he must have seen it: no thought of waiting to be attacked appears
  4250	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfrom first to last to have visited his subtle mind; he would not even
  4251	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thold off till the night was nearly spent; on he pounded with no policy
  4252	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbut to fall to [get into combat]. What could the bewildered scouts do,
  4253	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmasters as they were of every war-like artifice save this one, but trot
  4254	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thelplessly after him, exposing themselves fatally to view, while they
  4255	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgave pathetic utterance to the coyote cry.
  4256	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4257	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAround the brave Tiger Lily were a dozen of her stoutest warriors, and
  4258	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey suddenly saw the perfidious pirates bearing down upon them. Fell
  4259	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfrom their eyes then the film through which they had looked at
  4260	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tvictory. No more would they torture at the stake. For them the happy
  4261	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thunting-grounds was now. They knew it; but as their father’s sons they
  4262	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tacquitted themselves. Even then they had time to gather in a phalanx
  4263	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t[dense formation] that would have been hard to break had they risen
  4264	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tquickly, but this they were forbidden to do by the traditions of their
  4265	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\trace. It is written that the noble savage must never express surprise in
  4266	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe presence of the white. Thus terrible as the sudden appearance of the
  4267	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpirates must have been to them, they remained stationary for a moment,
  4268	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnot a muscle moving; as if the foe had come by invitation. Then, indeed,
  4269	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe tradition gallantly upheld, they seized their weapons, and the air
  4270	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas torn with the war-cry; but it was now too late.
  4271	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4272	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt is no part of ours to describe what was a massacre rather than a
  4273	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfight. Thus perished many of the flower of the Piccaninny tribe. Not all
  4274	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tunavenged did they die, for with Lean Wolf fell Alf Mason, to disturb
  4275	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe Spanish Main no more, and among others who bit the dust were Geo.
  4276	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tScourie, Chas. Turley, and the Alsatian Foggerty. Turley fell to the
  4277	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttomahawk of the terrible Panther, who ultimately cut a way through the
  4278	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpirates with Tiger Lily and a small remnant of the tribe.
  4279	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4280	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTo what extent Hook is to blame for his tactics on this occasion is for
  4281	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe historian to decide. Had he waited on the rising ground till the
  4282	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tproper hour he and his men would probably have been butchered; and in
  4283	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tjudging him it is only fair to take this into account. What he should
  4284	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tperhaps have done was to acquaint his opponents that he proposed to
  4285	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfollow a new method. On the other hand, this, as destroying the element
  4286	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof surprise, would have made his strategy of no avail, so that the whole
  4287	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tquestion is beset with difficulties. One cannot at least withhold a
  4288	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\treluctant admiration for the wit that had conceived so bold a scheme,
  4289	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand the fell [deadly] genius with which it was carried out.
  4290	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4291	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhat were his own feelings about himself at that triumphant moment?
  4292	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFain [gladly] would his dogs have known, as breathing heavily and wiping
  4293	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir cutlasses, they gathered at a discreet distance from his hook, and
  4294	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsquinted through their ferret eyes at this extraordinary man. Elation
  4295	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmust have been in his heart, but his face did not reflect it: ever a
  4296	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdark and solitary enigma, he stood aloof from his followers in spirit as
  4297	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin substance.
  4298	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4299	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe night’s work was not yet over, for it was not the redskins he had
  4300	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcome out to destroy; they were but the bees to be smoked, so that he
  4301	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshould get at the honey. It was Pan he wanted, Pan and Wendy and their
  4302	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tband, but chiefly Pan.
  4303	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4304	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter was such a small boy that one tends to wonder at the man’s hatred
  4305	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof him. True he had flung Hook’s arm to the crocodile, but even this
  4306	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand the increased insecurity of life to which it led, owing to
  4307	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe crocodile’s pertinacity [persistance], hardly account for a
  4308	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tvindictiveness so relentless and malignant. The truth is that there was
  4309	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta something about Peter which goaded the pirate captain to frenzy. It
  4310	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas not his courage, it was not his engaging appearance, it was not--.
  4311	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThere is no beating about the bush, for we know quite well what it was,
  4312	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand have got to tell. It was Peter’s cockiness.
  4313	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4314	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThis had got on Hook’s nerves; it made his iron claw twitch, and at
  4315	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnight it disturbed him like an insect. While Peter lived, the tortured
  4316	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tman felt that he was a lion in a cage into which a sparrow had come.
  4317	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4318	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe question now was how to get down the trees, or how to get his dogs
  4319	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdown? He ran his greedy eyes over them, searching for the thinnest
  4320	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tones. They wriggled uncomfortably, for they knew he would not scruple
  4321	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t[hesitate] to ram them down with poles.
  4322	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4323	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn the meantime, what of the boys? We have seen them at the first clang
  4324	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof the weapons, turned as it were into stone figures, open-mouthed,
  4325	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tall appealing with outstretched arms to Peter; and we return to them as
  4326	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttheir mouths close, and their arms fall to their sides. The pandemonium
  4327	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tabove has ceased almost as suddenly as it arose, passed like a fierce
  4328	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgust of wind; but they know that in the passing it has determined their
  4329	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfate.
  4330	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4331	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWhich side had won?
  4332	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4333	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe pirates, listening avidly at the mouths of the trees, heard the
  4334	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tquestion put by every boy, and alas, they also heard Peter’s answer.
  4335	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4336	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“If the redskins have won,” he said, “they will beat the tom-tom; it is
  4337	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\talways their sign of victory.”
  4338	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4339	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNow Smee had found the tom-tom, and was at that moment sitting on it.
  4340	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“You will never hear the tom-tom again,” he muttered, but inaudibly of
  4341	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcourse, for strict silence had been enjoined [urged]. To his amazement

  4343	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tunderstanding of the dreadful wickedness of the order. Never, probably,
  4344	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thad this simple man admired Hook so much.
  4345	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4346	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tTwice Smee beat upon the instrument, and then stopped to listen
  4347	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgleefully.
  4348	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4349	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“The tom-tom,” the miscreants heard Peter cry; “an Indian victory!”
  4350	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4351	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe doomed children answered with a cheer that was music to the black
  4352	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thearts above, and almost immediately they repeated their good-byes
  4353	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto Peter. This puzzled the pirates, but all their other feelings were
  4354	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tswallowed by a base delight that the enemy were about to come up the
  4355	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttrees. They smirked at each other and rubbed their hands. Rapidly and
  4356	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsilently Hook gave his orders: one man to each tree, and the others to
  4357	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tarrange themselves in a line two yards apart.
  4358	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4359	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4360	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4361	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4362	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 13 DO YOU BELIEVE IN FAIRIES?
  4363	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4364	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe more quickly this horror is disposed of the better. The first to
  4365	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\temerge from his tree was Curly. He rose out of it into the arms of
  4366	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tCecco, who flung him to Smee, who flung him to Starkey, who flung him to
  4367	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBill Jukes, who flung him to Noodler, and so he was tossed from one to
  4368	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tanother till he fell at the feet of the black pirate. All the boys were
  4369	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tplucked from their trees in this ruthless manner; and several of them
  4370	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twere in the air at a time, like bales of goods flung from hand to hand.
  4371	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4372	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tA different treatment was accorded to Wendy, who came last. With
  4373	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tironical politeness Hook raised his hat to her, and, offering her his
  4374	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tarm, escorted her to the spot where the others were being gagged. He
  4375	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdid it with such an air, he was so frightfully DISTINGUE [imposingly
  4376	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdistinguished], that she was too fascinated to cry out. She was only a
  4377	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlittle girl.
  4378	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4379	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPerhaps it is tell-tale to divulge that for a moment Hook entranced her,
  4380	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand we tell on her only because her slip led to strange results. Had she
  4381	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thaughtily unhanded him (and we should have loved to write it of her),
  4382	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe would have been hurled through the air like the others, and then
  4383	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHook would probably not have been present at the tying of the children;
  4384	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand had he not been at the tying he would not have discovered Slightly’s
  4385	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsecret, and without the secret he could not presently have made his foul
  4386	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tattempt on Peter’s life.
  4387	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4388	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey were tied to prevent their flying away, doubled up with their knees
  4389	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tclose to their ears; and for the trussing of them the black pirate had
  4390	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcut a rope into nine equal pieces. All went well until Slightly’s turn
  4391	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcame, when he was found to be like those irritating parcels that use up
  4392	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tall the string in going round and leave no tags [ends] with which to
  4393	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttie a knot. The pirates kicked him in their rage, just as you kick the
  4394	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tparcel (though in fairness you should kick the string); and strange
  4395	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto say it was Hook who told them to belay their violence. His lip was
  4396	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcurled with malicious triumph. While his dogs were merely sweating
  4397	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbecause every time they tried to pack the unhappy lad tight in one
  4398	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpart he bulged out in another, Hook’s master mind had gone far beneath
  4399	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSlightly’s surface, probing not for effects but for causes; and his
  4400	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\texultation showed that he had found them. Slightly, white to the gills,
  4401	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tknew that Hook had surprised [discovered] his secret, which was this,
  4402	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat no boy so blown out could use a tree wherein an average man need
  4403	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstick. Poor Slightly, most wretched of all the children now, for he
  4404	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas in a panic about Peter, bitterly regretted what he had done. Madly
  4405	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\taddicted to the drinking of water when he was hot, he had swelled in
  4406	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tconsequence to his present girth, and instead of reducing himself to fit
  4407	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\this tree he had, unknown to the others, whittled his tree to make it fit
  4408	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thim.
  4409	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4410	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSufficient of this Hook guessed to persuade him that Peter at last lay
  4411	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat his mercy, but no word of the dark design that now formed in the
  4412	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsubterranean caverns of his mind crossed his lips; he merely signed
  4413	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat the captives were to be conveyed to the ship, and that he would be
  4414	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\talone.
  4415	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4416	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHow to convey them? Hunched up in their ropes they might indeed be
