  4690	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 14 THE PIRATE SHIP
  4691	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4692	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOne green light squinting over Kidd’s Creek, which is near the mouth of
  4693	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe pirate river, marked where the brig, the JOLLY ROGER, lay, low in
  4694	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe water; a rakish-looking [speedy-looking] craft foul to the hull,
  4695	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tevery beam in her detestable, like ground strewn with mangled feathers.
  4696	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe was the cannibal of the seas, and scarce needed that watchful eye,
  4697	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfor she floated immune in the horror of her name.
  4698	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4699	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe was wrapped in the blanket of night, through which no sound from her
  4700	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcould have reached the shore. There was little sound, and none agreeable
  4701	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsave the whir of the ship’s sewing machine at which Smee sat, ever
  4702	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tindustrious and obliging, the essence of the commonplace, pathetic Smee.
  4703	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tI know not why he was so infinitely pathetic, unless it were because
  4704	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\the was so pathetically unaware of it; but even strong men had to turn
  4705	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thastily from looking at him, and more than once on summer evenings he
  4706	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thad touched the fount of Hook’s tears and made it flow. Of this, as of
  4707	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\talmost everything else, Smee was quite unconscious.
  4708	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4709	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tA few of the pirates leant over the bulwarks, drinking in the miasma
  4710	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t[putrid mist] of the night; others sprawled by barrels over games of
  4711	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdice and cards; and the exhausted four who had carried the little house
  4712	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlay prone on the deck, where even in their sleep they rolled skillfully
  4713	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto this side or that out of Hook’s reach, lest he should claw them
  4714	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmechanically in passing.
  4715	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4716	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHook trod the deck in thought. O man unfathomable. It was his hour of
  4717	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttriumph. Peter had been removed for ever from his path, and all the
  4718	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tother boys were in the brig, about to walk the plank. It was his

  4979	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  4982	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4983	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4984	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4985	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4986	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 15 “HOOK OR ME THIS TIME”
  4987	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4988	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOdd things happen to all of us on our way through life without our
  4989	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnoticing for a time that they have happened. Thus, to take an instance,
  4990	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twe suddenly discover that we have been deaf in one ear for we don’t know
  4991	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thow long, but, say, half an hour. Now such an experience had come that
  4992	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnight to Peter. When last we saw him he was stealing across the island
  4993	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith one finger to his lips and his dagger at the ready. He had seen the
  4994	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcrocodile pass by without noticing anything peculiar about it, but by
  4995	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand by he remembered that it had not been ticking. At first he thought
  4996	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthis eerie, but soon concluded rightly that the clock had run down.
  4997	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  4998	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWithout giving a thought to what might be the feelings of a
  4999	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfellow-creature thus abruptly deprived of its closest companion, Peter
  5000	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbegan to consider how he could turn the catastrophe to his own use;
  5001	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand he decided to tick, so that wild beasts should believe he was the
  5002	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcrocodile and let him pass unmolested. He ticked superbly, but with one
  5003	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tunforeseen result. The crocodile was among those who heard the sound,
  5004	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand it followed him, though whether with the purpose of regaining what
  5005	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit had lost, or merely as a friend under the belief that it was again
  5006	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tticking itself, will never be certainly known, for, like slaves to a
  5007	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfixed idea, it was a stupid beast.
  5008	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5009	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter reached the shore without mishap, and went straight on, his legs
  5010	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tencountering the water as if quite unaware that they had entered a new
  5011	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\telement. Thus many animals pass from land to water, but no other human
  5012	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof whom I know. As he swam he had but one thought: “Hook or me this
  5013	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttime.” He had ticked so long that he now went on ticking without knowing
  5014	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat he was doing it. Had he known he would have stopped, for to board
  5015	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe brig by help of the tick, though an ingenious idea, had not occurred
  5016	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto him.
  5017	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5018	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOn the contrary, he thought he had scaled her side as noiseless as a
  5019	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmouse; and he was amazed to see the pirates cowering from him, with Hook
  5020	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin their midst as abject as if he had heard the crocodile.
  5021	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5022	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe crocodile! No sooner did Peter remember it than he heard the
  5023	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tticking. At first he thought the sound did come from the crocodile,
  5024	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand he looked behind him swiftly. Then he realised that he was doing it
  5025	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thimself, and in a flash he understood the situation. “How clever of me!”

  5330	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat them impotently, his mind was no longer with them; it was slouching
  5331	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin the playing fields of long ago, or being sent up [to the headmaster]
  5332	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfor good, or watching the wall-game from a famous wall. And his shoes
  5333	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twere right, and his waistcoat was right, and his tie was right, and his
  5334	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsocks were right.
  5335	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5336	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tJames Hook, thou not wholly unheroic figure, farewell.
  5337	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5338	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tFor we have come to his last moment.
  5339	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5340	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSeeing Peter slowly advancing upon him through the air with dagger
  5341	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpoised, he sprang upon the bulwarks to cast himself into the sea. He
  5342	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdid not know that the crocodile was waiting for him; for we purposely
  5343	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstopped the clock that this knowledge might be spared him: a little mark
  5344	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof respect from us at the end.
  5345	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5346	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tHe had one last triumph, which I think we need not grudge him. As he
  5347	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstood on the bulwark looking over his shoulder at Peter gliding through
  5348	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe air, he invited him with a gesture to use his foot. It made Peter
  5349	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tkick instead of stab.
  5350	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5351	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAt last Hook had got the boon for which he craved.
  5352	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5353	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Bad form,” he cried jeeringly, and went content to the crocodile.
  5354	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5355	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThus perished James Hook.
  5356	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5357	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Seventeen,” Slightly sang out; but he was not quite correct in his
  5358	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfigures. Fifteen paid the penalty for their crimes that night; but two
  5359	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\treached the shore: Starkey to be captured by the redskins, who made him

  5361	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSmee, who henceforth wandered about the world in his spectacles, making
  5362	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta precarious living by saying he was the only man that Jas. Hook had
  5363	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfeared.
  5364	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5365	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy, of course, had stood by taking no part in the fight, though

  5369	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand then she took them into Hook’s cabin and pointed to his watch which
  5370	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twas hanging on a nail. It said “half-past one!”
  5371	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  5376	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcried in his sleep for a long time, and Wendy held him tightly.
  5377	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5378	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5379	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5380	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5381	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tChapter 16 THE RETURN HOME
  5382	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5383	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tBy three bells that morning they were all stirring their stumps [legs];
  5384	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfor there was a big sea running; and Tootles, the bo’sun, was among
  5385	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem, with a rope’s end in his hand and chewing tobacco. They all donned
  5386	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpirate clothes cut off at the knee, shaved smartly, and tumbled up, with
  5387	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe true nautical roll and hitching their trousers.
  5388	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5389	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt need not be said who was the captain. Nibs and John were first and
  5390	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsecond mate. There was a woman aboard. The rest were tars [sailors]
  5391	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbefore the mast, and lived in the fo’c’sle. Peter had already lashed
  5392	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thimself to the wheel; but he piped all hands and delivered a short
  5393	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\taddress to them; said he hoped they would do their duty like gallant
  5394	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thearties, but that he knew they were the scum of Rio and the Gold Coast,
  5395	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand if they snapped at him he would tear them. The bluff strident words
  5396	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tstruck the note sailors understood, and they cheered him lustily. Then
  5397	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta few sharp orders were given, and they turned the ship round, and nosed
  5398	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther for the mainland.
  5399	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5400	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tCaptain Pan calculated, after consulting the ship’s chart, that if this
  5401	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tweather lasted they should strike the Azores about the 21st of June,
  5402	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tafter which it would save time to fly.
  5403	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5404	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tSome of them wanted it to be an honest ship and others were in favour
  5405	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof keeping it a pirate; but the captain treated them as dogs, and they
  5406	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdared not express their wishes to him even in a round robin [one person
  5407	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tafter another, as they had to Cpt. Hook]. Instant obedience was the only
  5408	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsafe thing. Slightly got a dozen for looking perplexed when told to take
  5409	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsoundings. The general feeling was that Peter was honest just now to
  5410	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlull Wendy’s suspicions, but that there might be a change when the new
  5411	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsuit was ready, which, against her will, she was making for him out of
  5412	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsome of Hook’s wickedest garments. It was afterwards whispered among
  5413	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem that on the first night he wore this suit he sat long in the cabin
  5414	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twith Hook’s cigar-holder in his mouth and one hand clenched, all but for
  5415	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe forefinger, which he bent and held threateningly aloft like a hook.
  5416	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5417	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tInstead of watching the ship, however, we must now return to that
  5418	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdesolate home from which three of our characters had taken heartless
  5419	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tflight so long ago. It seems a shame to have neglected No. 14 all this
  5420	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttime; and yet we may be sure that Mrs. Darling does not blame us. If we
  5421	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thad returned sooner to look with sorrowful sympathy at her, she would
  5422	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tprobably have cried, “Don’t be silly; what do I matter? Do go back and
  5423	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tkeep an eye on the children.” So long as mothers are like this their
  5424	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tchildren will take advantage of them; and they may lay to [bet on] that.
  5425	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5426	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tEven now we venture into that familiar nursery only because its lawful
  5427	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\toccupants are on their way home; we are merely hurrying on in advance
  5428	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof them to see that their beds are properly aired and that Mr. and Mrs.
  5429	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDarling do not go out for the evening. We are no more than servants. Why
  5430	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ton earth should their beds be properly aired, seeing that they left them
  5431	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin such a thankless hurry? Would it not serve them jolly well right if
  5432	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey came back and found that their parents were spending the week-end
  5433	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tin the country? It would be the moral lesson they have been in need
  5434	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof ever since we met them; but if we contrived things in this way Mrs.
  5435	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDarling would never forgive us.
  5436	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5437	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOne thing I should like to do immensely, and that is to tell her, in the
  5438	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tway authors have, that the children are coming back, that indeed they
  5439	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twill be here on Thursday week. This would spoil so completely the
  5440	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsurprise to which Wendy and John and Michael are looking forward. They
  5441	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thave been planning it out on the ship: mother’s rapture, father’s shout
  5442	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tof joy, Nana’s leap through the air to embrace them first, when what
  5443	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthey ought to be prepared for is a good hiding. How delicious to spoil
  5444	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tit all by breaking the news in advance; so that when they enter grandly
  5445	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMrs. Darling may not even offer Wendy her mouth, and Mr. Darling may
  5446	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\texclaim pettishly, “Dash it all, here are those boys again.” However,
  5447	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twe should get no thanks even for this. We are beginning to know Mrs.
  5448	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDarling by this time, and may be sure that she would upbraid us for
  5449	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdepriving the children of their little pleasure.
  5450	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5451	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“But, my dear madam, it is ten days till Thursday week; so that by
  5452	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttelling you what’s what, we can save you ten days of unhappiness.”
  5453	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5454	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes, but at what a cost! By depriving the children of ten minutes of
  5455	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdelight.”
  5456	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5457	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Oh, if you look at it in that way!”
  5458	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5459	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“What other way is there in which to look at it?”
  5460	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5461	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tYou see, the woman had no proper spirit. I had meant to say
  5462	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\textraordinarily nice things about her; but I despise her, and not one of
  5463	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem will I say now. She does not really need to be told to have things
  5464	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tready, for they are ready. All the beds are aired, and she never leaves
  5465	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe house, and observe, the window is open. For all the use we are to
  5466	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther, we might well go back to the ship. However, as we are here we may
  5467	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tas well stay and look on. That is all we are, lookers-on. Nobody really
  5468	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twants us. So let us watch and say jaggy things, in the hope that some of
  5469	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthem will hurt.
  5470	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5471	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThe only change to be seen in the night-nursery is that between nine
  5472	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand six the kennel is no longer there. When the children flew away, Mr.
  5473	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tDarling felt in his bones that all the blame was his for having chained
  5474	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNana up, and that from first to last she had been wiser than he. Of
  5475	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcourse, as we have seen, he was quite a simple man; indeed he might have
  5476	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpassed for a boy again if he had been able to take his baldness off;
  5477	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbut he had also a noble sense of justice and a lion’s courage to do what
  5478	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tseemed right to him; and having thought the matter out with anxious care
  5479	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tafter the flight of the children, he went down on all fours and crawled
  5480	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tinto the kennel. To all Mrs. Darling’s dear invitations to him to come
  5481	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tout he replied sadly but firmly:
  5482	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5483	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“No, my own one, this is the place for me.”
  5484	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5485	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIn the bitterness of his remorse he swore that he would never leave
  5486	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe kennel until his children came back. Of course this was a pity; but
  5487	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhatever Mr. Darling did he had to do in excess, otherwise he soon gave
  5488	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tup doing it. And there never was a more humble man than the once proud
  5489	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tGeorge Darling, as he sat in the kennel of an evening talking with his
  5490	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twife of their children and all their pretty ways.
  5491	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5492	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tVery touching was his deference to Nana. He would not let her come into
  5493	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe kennel, but on all other matters he followed her wishes implicitly.
  5494	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5495	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tEvery morning the kennel was carried with Mr. Darling in it to a cab,
  5496	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhich conveyed him to his office, and he returned home in the same way
  5497	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat six. Something of the strength of character of the man will be seen
  5498	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tif we remember how sensitive he was to the opinion of neighbours: this
  5499	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tman whose every movement now attracted surprised attention. Inwardly he
  5500	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmust have suffered torture; but he preserved a calm exterior even when
  5501	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe young criticised his little home, and he always lifted his hat
  5502	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcourteously to any lady who looked inside.
  5503	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5504	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tIt may have been Quixotic, but it was magnificent. Soon the inward
  5505	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmeaning of it leaked out, and the great heart of the public was touched.
  5506	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tCrowds followed the cab, cheering it lustily; charming girls scaled it
  5507	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tto get his autograph; interviews appeared in the better class of papers,
  5508	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand society invited him to dinner and added, “Do come in the kennel.”
  5509	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5510	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOn that eventful Thursday week, Mrs. Darling was in the night-nursery
  5511	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tawaiting George’s return home; a very sad-eyed woman. Now that we look
  5512	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tat her closely and remember the gaiety of her in the old days, all gone
  5513	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tnow just because she has lost her babes, I find I won’t be able to say

  5515	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tchildren, she couldn’t help it. Look at her in her chair, where she has
  5516	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfallen asleep. The corner of her mouth, where one looks first, is almost
  5517	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twithered up. Her hand moves restlessly on her breast as if she had a
  5518	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpain there. Some like Peter best, and some like Wendy best, but I like
  5519	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ther best. Suppose, to make her happy, we whisper to her in her sleep
  5520	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat the brats are coming back. They are really within two miles of the
  5521	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twindow now, and flying strong, but all we need whisper is that they are
  5522	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ton the way. Let’s.

  5949	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5950	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tPeter came next spring cleaning; and the strange thing was that he never
  5951	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tknew he had missed a year.
  5952	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5953	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThat was the last time the girl Wendy ever saw him. For a little longer
  5954	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe tried for his sake not to have growing pains; and she felt she was
  5955	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tuntrue to him when she got a prize for general knowledge. But the years
  5956	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcame and went without bringing the careless boy; and when they met again
  5957	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy was a married woman, and Peter was no more to her than a little
  5958	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdust in the box in which she had kept her toys. Wendy was grown up. You
  5959	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tneed not be sorry for her. She was one of the kind that likes to grow
  5960	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tup. In the end she grew up of her own free will a day quicker than other
  5961	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tgirls.
  5962	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5963	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAll the boys were grown up and done for by this time; so it is scarcely
  5964	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tworth while saying anything more about them. You may see the twins and
  5965	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tNibs and Curly any day going to an office, each carrying a little bag
  5966	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand an umbrella. Michael is an engine-driver [train engineer]. Slightly
  5967	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmarried a lady of title, and so he became a lord. You see that judge in
  5968	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ta wig coming out at the iron door? That used to be Tootles. The bearded
  5969	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tman who doesn’t know any story to tell his children was once John.
  5970	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5971	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tWendy was married in white with a pink sash. It is strange to think
  5972	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthat Peter did not alight in the church and forbid the banns [formal
  5973	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tannouncement of a marriage].
  5974	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5975	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tYears rolled on again, and Wendy had a daughter. This ought not to be
  5976	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twritten in ink but in a golden splash.
  5977	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5978	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe was called Jane, and always had an odd inquiring look, as if from
  5979	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe moment she arrived on the mainland she wanted to ask questions. When
  5980	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tshe was old enough to ask them they were mostly about Peter Pan. She
  5981	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tloved to hear of Peter, and Wendy told her all she could remember in the
  5982	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tvery nursery from which the famous flight had taken place. It was
  5983	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tJane’s nursery now, for her father had bought it at the three per cents
  5984	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t[mortgage rate] from Wendy’s father, who was no longer fond of stairs.
  5985	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tMrs. Darling was now dead and forgotten.
  5986	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5987	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThere were only two beds in the nursery now, Jane’s and her nurse’s; and
  5988	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthere was no kennel, for Nana also had passed away. She died of old age,
  5989	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand at the end she had been rather difficult to get on with; being very
  5990	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tfirmly convinced that no one knew how to look after children except
  5991	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\therself.
  5992	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5993	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOnce a week Jane’s nurse had her evening off; and then it was Wendy’s
  5994	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpart to put Jane to bed. That was the time for stories. It was Jane’s
  5995	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tinvention to raise the sheet over her mother’s head and her own, thus
  5996	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tmaking a tent, and in the awful darkness to whisper:
  5997	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  5998	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“What do we see now?”
  5999	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6000	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I don’t think I see anything to-night,” says Wendy, with a feeling that
  6001	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tif Nana were here she would object to further conversation.
  6002	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6003	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes, you do,” says Jane, “you see when you were a little girl.”
  6004	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6005	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“That is a long time ago, sweetheart,” says Wendy. “Ah me, how time
  6006	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tflies!”
  6007	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6008	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Does it fly,” asks the artful child, “the way you flew when you were a
  6009	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlittle girl?”
  6010	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6011	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“The way I flew? Do you know, Jane, I sometimes wonder whether I ever
  6012	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdid really fly.”
  6013	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6014	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes, you did.”
  6015	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6016	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“The dear old days when I could fly!”
  6017	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6018	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Why can’t you fly now, mother?”
  6019	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6020	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Because I am grown up, dearest. When people grow up they forget the
  6021	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tway.”
  6022	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6023	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Why do they forget the way?”
  6024	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6025	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Because they are no longer gay and innocent and heartless. It is only
  6026	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe gay and innocent and heartless who can fly.”
  6027	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6028	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“What is gay and innocent and heartless? I do wish I were gay and
  6029	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tinnocent and heartless.”
  6030	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6031	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tOr perhaps Wendy admits she does see something.
  6032	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6033	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I do believe,” she says, “that it is this nursery.”
  6034	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6035	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I do believe it is,” says Jane. “Go on.”
  6036	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6037	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tThey are now embarked on the great adventure of the night when Peter
  6038	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tflew in looking for his shadow.
  6039	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6040	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“The foolish fellow,” says Wendy, “tried to stick it on with soap, and
  6041	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhen he could not he cried, and that woke me, and I sewed it on for
  6042	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\thim.”
  6043	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6044	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“You have missed a bit,” interrupts Jane, who now knows the story better
  6045	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthan her mother. “When you saw him sitting on the floor crying, what did
  6046	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tyou say?”
  6047	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6048	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“I sat up in bed and I said, ‘Boy, why are you crying?’”
  6049	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6050	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes, that was it,” says Jane, with a big breath.
  6051	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6052	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“And then he flew us all away to the Neverland and the fairies and the
  6053	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tpirates and the redskins and the mermaids’ lagoon, and the home under
  6054	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe ground, and the little house.”
  6055	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6056	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t“Yes! which did you like best of all?”
  6057	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  6059	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  6061	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  6063	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tand then some night you will hear me crowing.’”

  6068	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tas grown up as that.
  6069	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  6073	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  6075	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tever so much better than her mother.
  6076	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  6082	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tone who heard it awake.”

  6085	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6086	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tAnd then one night came the tragedy. It was the spring of the year, and
  6087	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthe story had been told for the night, and Jane was now asleep in her
  6088	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tbed. Wendy was sitting on the floor, very close to the fire, so as to
  6089	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tsee to darn, for there was no other light in the nursery; and while she

  6094	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tall his first teeth.

  6100	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tthinking chiefly of himself; and in the dim light her white dress might

  6151	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t
  6152	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tShe let her hands play in the hair of the tragic boy. She was not a
  6153	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tlittle girl heart-broken about him; she was a grown woman smiling at it

  6157	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\twhen the tall beautiful creature stooped to lift him in her arms he drew

  6159	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\t

  6233	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tuntil they were as small as stars.

  6237	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcommon grown-up, with a daughter called Margaret; and every spring
  6238	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tcleaning time, except when he forgets, Peter comes for Margaret and
  6239	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\ttakes her to the Neverland, where she tells him stories about himself,

  6241	PeterPanbyJMBarrie.txt\tdaughter, who is to be Peter’s mother in turn; and thus it will go on,
